3 BASELINE (SECTION B-PART 2)
3.1 Vertebrate Animals (wildlife)
3.1.1 Introduction
This report provides the results of the baseline characterization of fauna (animals
wild) associated with the Mining Development Project for the Extraction of Clays for the Production of
Rare Earth Concentrate (hereinafter “the Project”), which will be located in the communes of Penco
(mostly) and Concepción, Province of Concepción, Biobío Region, and its sensitivity in relation
with the planned activities and works. It should be noted that, for the preparation of this report,
followed the guidelines established in the “Guide for the Description of the area of Influence: Description
of the Soil, Flora and Fauna Components of Terrestrial Ecosystems in the SEIA (SEA 2015), the “Guide on
the Area of Influence in the Environmental Impact Assessment System” (SEA 2017) and the Guide “Criteria of
Evaluation in the SEIA: Technical Criteria for Terrestrial Fauna Land Campaigns and Validation of
data” (SEA 2022).
In this way, the characterization of the component was carried out based on the requirements of the System
of Environmental Impact Assessment, within the framework of what is established by Law No. 19,300 on Bases
General Environmental Regulations and their subsequent modifications, and specifically, letter e.2) of the
Article 18 of the D.S. N°40, of 2012, modified by D.S. N°30, from 2023, both from the Ministry of the Environment
Environment, which modified the Regulation of the Environmental Impact Assessment System (RSEIA).
3.1.2 Objectives
3.1.2.1 General Objective
The objective of this report is to identify the presence of environmental singularities that represent
critical variables for the fauna component in relation to the execution of the Project, based on the
surveys and analysis of resident wildlife.
3.1.2.2 Specific Objectives
• Identify the environments related to fauna present in the area of influence of the Project.
• Recognize the existence of sites of importance for fauna.
• Identify the total richness of fauna species, for each registered environment.
3-1
==================================================================================
PAGE 17
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
• Classify the registered fauna species according to the conservation category criteria,
origin, mobility, immigration status and BSE (Protection criteria indicated in Article 3 of
Law No. 19,473/1996 and its regulations D.S. N°05/1998).
• Determine the estimated abundance and relative abundance of the fauna recorded by environment.
• Estimate the biological diversity of the community and/or environments through the Shannon index
- Wiener and Simpson.
• Evaluate the validity of the design and sampling effort, based on the construction of a curve of
rarefaction.
• Identify sensitive and/or interesting fauna species, according to their category of
conservation and/or its potential impact by the Project.
3.1.3 Area of Influence
The area of influence of the Project is located within the communes of Penco and Concepción, in the province
of Concepción, Biobío Region. The area where the Project will be located corresponds to a sector located
east of Route 150 and also crosses Route 152 in the central area of the area; the project has a
apr extension
approximated 3.0 km by 2.5 km, and an intervention area of ​​169.19 ha. For the purposes of this
baseline study, this area includes a buffer area considering an approximate width of
200 m or more from the limit of the Project areas towards the outside, forming a polygon with a
total area of 673.62 hectares (Figure 3-1). This buffer area is larger than the intervention area of the
Project, since it considers an additional surface that could potentially be affected by the
development of the Project works and activities, considering the inherent displacement capacity
that has the fauna component.
For the definition and description of the area of influence of the terrestrial vertebrate fauna component,
followed the guidelines of the “Guide for the description of the soil, flora and fauna components of
terrestrial ecosystems in the SEIA” (SEA 2015) and the “Guide for the description of the area of influence in the
SEIA” (SEA 2017), which consider the following aspects: preliminary identification of possible
effects, spatial extension of the parts, works and/or activities of the Project, the geographical space of the
elements receiving impacts, relevant attributes and current situation. Once these are defined
aspects, the area of influence was established where the effects on the component can manifest
fauna product of the parts and works of the Project for its different phases. Annex 3B-33 presents
the AI of the component in digital format.

3.1.4.1 Pre-ground cabinet
i. Determination and description of environments present in the project works
Based on the information collected on the ground and the photointerpretive analyzes of the formations
vegetation of the flora and vegetation component, the environments for the fauna present were determined
in the area of influence. Based on the above, criteria were used to define the
environments: vegetation characteristics, the presence of bodies of water and the presence of activity
anthropic.

definition. Then, Table 3-2 shows the surfaces of each environment and their representation.
percentage of the total.

ii. Identification of species and potential sites of interest for fauna
With the objective of directing sampling efforts in the field, potential species were determined.
for the area of influence of the Project based on a bibliographic review. Bibliographic sources
used to determine the potential fauna were the following:
• Reptiles and Amphibians: Cei (1962), Donoso-Barros (1966), Pincheira-Donoso & Núñez (2005), Rabanal
& Núñez (2008), Vidal & Labra (2008), Demangel (2016), Mella (2017) and Charrier (2019).
• Birds: Marín (2004), Muñoz-Pedreros et al. (2004), Araya & Millie (2005), Jaramillo (2005), Chester
(2008), Couve et al. (2016), Martínez & González (2017) and Medrano et al. (2018).
• Mammals: Mann (1978), Iriarte (2008), Muñoz-Pedreros & Yánez (2009), Ossa et al. (2010),
Rodríguez-San Pedro & Simonetti (2013), Rodríguez-San Pedro et al. (2016), Iriarte & Jaksic (2022)
and Galaz et al. (2020).
iii. Sampling design and sampling unit
in ba
For the preliminary identification with photointerpretation of the indicated environments, the
systematic stratified sampling, establishing the fauna sampling point as the sampling unit
(PMF). The establishment of the PMF considered a distance of 500 to 1,500 linear meters between
points and a maximum surface of 20,000 m2 (varying according to the taxa to be sampled) in order to
provide adequate spatial representation to the entire area of influence of the Project.
iv. Field activities
a) Identification of sites of interest for fauna
During the field surveys, a search was carried out for sites of interest for fauna within
of the area of influence. A site of interest was considered as one that presents characteristics
limiting or restrictive for one or more species, or provides resources that are difficult to replace and/or
particular conditions associated with it or with singular species.
b) Sampling by class: techniques and methods
A systematic sampling of the entire area of influence was carried out, focused on richness and abundance
of fauna species, oriented towards species of terrestrial vertebrates of the following groups
taxonomic: Amphibians, Reptiles, Birds and Mammals (which includes micromammals and bats), noting
their presence based on sightings, vocalizations, and/or indirect records (for example, feces,
hairs, footprints, pellets or nests). In general terms, the area of influence was toured, paying special attention to
emphasis on the different environments or microhabitats, where the observed specimens were identified
and photographed. The relative abundance was estimated for each class, as well as the proportion between the
counted individuals of the species and the total number of counted individuals of each class.
3-9
==================================================================================
PAGE 25
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Additionally, according to the information collected, the diversity (richness of
species) and density (number of individuals/ha) in the area of influence.
In each PMF, specific methods were implemented for the detection of potential taxa in the area.
of influence, based on what is indicated by the guidelines of the Agricultural and Livestock Service (SAG 2012a, 2012b,
2016) and the Environmental Evaluation Service (SEA, 2015). Below are the techniques
methodologies carried out by class, while the details of these in each PMF are presented in the section
of Sampling Effort.
Amphibia
Transects: pedestrian routes were carried out in which the presence of amphibians was searched through
visual evidence, within the limits given by the observer's detection capacity, which is
estimated at three meters on each side of the line of progression. Each transect had a duration of 15
average minutes and a length of 400 meters, covering a total area of 2,400 m2.
Playback: with the aim of encouraging the singing of the specimens present in the area of influence,
used the methodology of playback or reproduction of the songs of the species with potential presence in
the area, for 0.5 hours in the evening and/or night at each sampling station that had the
conditions for this taxon. Species recorded using this method were excluded from the calculation
of densities, due to the impossibility of obtaining precise data on the number of specimens
(Sutherland 2006), so it was only used as an indicator of presence.
Directed search: in attention to the habits of the potential species for the area of influence, the
Carrying out transects was complemented with the directed search for amphibians in potential refuges,
which consisted of lifting stones, searching within the vegetation for sectors with water and
other possible refuges.
Reptilia
Transects: pedestrian routes were carried out in which the presence of reptiles was searched through
visual evidence, within the limits given by the observer's detection capacity, which is
estimated at three meters on each side of the line of progression. Each transect had a duration of 15
average minutes and a length of 400 meters, covering a total area of 2,400 m2.
Targeted search: e
n attention to the habits of the potential species for the area of influence, the
The realization of transects was complemented with the directed search for reptiles in potential refuges,
which consisted of the systematic lifting of stones, logs and other possible shelters such as
bushes.

==================================================================================
PAGE 26
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Birds
Specific station: observation points were made in which the visual record was carried out, with the
help of binoculars (10x25), and the recognition of bird vocalizations. At each point
waited five minutes to stabilize the birds' behavior upon reaching each sector (Reynolds et
al. 1980) and later the count began. Each point had a duration of 10 minutes and a radius of
50 meters, covering a total area of 7,850 m2.
Playback: with the aim of encouraging the singing of the specimens present in the area of influence,
used the methodology of playback or reproduction of the songs of nocturnal species with presence
potential in the area, for 0.5 hours in the evening and/or night at each sampling station. The
Species recorded using this method were excluded from the calculation of densities, due to the
impossibility of obtaining precise data on the number of specimens (Sutherland 2006), so it is only
used as an indicator of presence.
Directed search: complementary, considering the habits of the potential species for the
area of influence, all direct and indirect evidence was searched (nests, feathers, pellets,
footprints, etc.) that could be observed in the area of influence.
Mammalia
Transects: pedestrian routes were carried out in which all direct evidence and
indirect (footprints, feces, hair, burrows, bone remains, etc.) that were observed on the ground within
of the limits given by the observer's detection capacity, which was estimated at 25 meters at each
side of the line of progression. Each transect had an average duration of 15 minutes and a length of
400 meters, covering a total area of 20,000 m2.
Camera traps: camera traps were installed (Bushnell®: Trophy Camera Brown, Model 119436 and
Bushnell®: Trophy Camera Brown HD, Model 119537C). This type of equipment consists of a sensor
movement that detects the individual in the environment and takes a photograph of it. The cameras were
baited with horse mackerel and wet cat food to attract carnivores.
With the objective of obtaining information on meso-mammals present in the area of influence of the project,
and characterize them ecologically, camera traps were installed in combination with olfactory stations.
The methodology consists of the installation of motion-sensitive cameras such as Bushnell Tophy Cam,
along with the use of a bait (attractor), in places close to possible transit sites for species of the
target group, such as trails or bodies of water, thus managing to photographically capture more species
elusive or less frequent. A total of 26 cameras were available between July 2022 and July 2022.
2023, which were monitored with semiannual campaigns, totaling a total of 1,288 traps-
night. Table 4-3 and Figure 4-2 of Annex 3B-38 show the location of each of the cameras.
trap installed.
3-11
==================================================================================
PAGE 27
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Sherman traps: to capture rodents and/or marsupials, Sherman-type traps were installed.
which were barley with crushed oats and vanilla essence. The traps were installed in grids in
groups of 25 traps, separated by 10 m from each other, giving rise to five parallel lines of 50 m
long and 10 m wide (2,500 m2). The sampling effort was three nights of capture at each point.
sampling per campaign carried out.
Additionally, for the potential micromammal species Dromiciops gliroi
des (monito del monte)
carried out specific monitoring with a field campaign between March 20 and 25, 2023, with the
objective of determining the presence of the species in different sectors of the Project's area of influence.
6 wildlife specialists participated in the field campaign, who visited different
sectors to identify potential habitats of the species. During the field visit, a total of 240
hours of field work. In this way, 9 monitoring stations were defined where
25 Sherman traps per specific site. Details are presented in Table 1-1 and Figure 1-3 of Annex 3B-39.
of the location of the specific sampling sites.
The captures carried out were carried out in accordance with the authorization of the Department of Protection of
Renewable Natural Resources of the Agricultural and Livestock Service through the Exempt Resolution
N°360/2022, valid between May 16, 2022 and May 16, 2023; and Exempt Resolutions
N°1025/2023 and 1026/2023 valid between August 18, 2023 and August 18, 2024. In the Annex
3B-32 a copy of all Exempt Resolutions is attached.
Ultrasonic detector: passive sampling stations were installed to record bats,
through the ultrasound recording carried out by a Bat detector equipment (Song Meter SM3BAT and SM4BAT,
Wildlife Acoustics). The recordings were made from sunset (6:00 p.m. to 9:00 p.m.) until
dawn (07:00 or 08:00 hrs), remaining active for 12 or 13 hours at each sampling site depending on
the climatic season. With the Kaleidoscope software (version 5.1.9), the pulses that could
correspond to bat calls and the following parameters were manually obtained: i)
initial frequency, ii) final frequency, iii) frequency of maximum intensity, iv) duration, v) interval between
pulses and vi) slope. These were processed and analyzed, comparing them with sonograms of
reference (Ossa et al. 2010, Rodríguez-San Pedro & Simonetti 2013, Rodríguez-San Pedro et al. 2016) for
identify the bats present in the area at the species level.
c) Sampling effort
Five field campaigns were carried out, the first between June 13 and 17, 2022 (autumn); the
second between August 15 and 19, 2022 (winter); the third between October 3 and 7, 2022
(spring); the fourth between January 9 and 13, 2023 (summer); and the fifth between the 2nd and 6th of
October 2023 (spring). Each work day lasted approximately 10 effective hours.
and had the presence of three teams of two specialists in wildlife studies in each
field campaign, totaling around 300 hours of work per field campaign.

Species classification
Based on the information obtained in the field, the recorded species were classified according to their
conservation status, biogeographic origin, mobility, migratory status and BSE criteria (B: beneficial
for forestry and agricultural activity, S: species cataloged with reduced population densities and E:
species cataloged as beneficial for maintaining the balance of natural ecosystems).

national legislation (Table 3-5). For the purposes of this report and in accordance with what is stated in
the document “Priority for the purposes of the SEIA of the Classifications and/or Categorizations of species of
wild flora and fauna” (CONAMA, 2008), to cite the conservation category of a species,
consider the following lists:

v. Determination of ecological parameters
a) Wealth
From the results obtained in the PMF, the specific richness was calculated, which corresponds to the
number of species identified and counted per sampling unit. This calculation was carried out
general way for the entire area, by environment and by class.
b) Absolute abundance
Absolute abundance is the expression of the abundance of a species. It should be noted that it refers to the
number of individuals counted during a sampling and does not represent the total number of individuals belonging
to a population of a given area (SEA, 2015).
c) Relative abundance
Relative abundance is the expression of the abundance of one species in relation to another in magnitude.
(SEA 2015). In this case it is expressed as the number of individuals of a species, based on the total number of
individuals recorded for all species. For this calculation, the species whose records
were carried out through direct observation (transects and captures), therefore, records will be excluded
indirect effects raised by playback, camera traps, bat detectors, among others. The calculation of abundance
relative is given below (Equation 3-1):
Equation 3-1. Relative abundance.
Number of individuals of species x
Relative abundance =( )∗100
Number of individuals of all species
d) Estimated density
From the absolute abundance records in each PMF, the number of individuals of a
same species present in a defined area unit. For the purposes of this study, it was calculated
individuals/hectares (ind/ha), to subsequently obtain the average estimated densities. The
indirect records (such as feces, footprints, feathers, etc.) and records of certain methodological techniques
that allow obtaining only wealth, were not considered in these calculations.
The calculations considered by group are those indicated in the following equations (Equation 3-2, Equation
3-3, Equation 3-4 and Equation 3-5):

Individuals Average number of individuals per environment

e) Diversity
The communities identified in the area of influence were characterized through the main
alpha or local biodiversity indicators (Shannon and Simpson index) (Moreno 2001). The objective is
to be able to have a first approximation of the biodiversity of the Project area at a global level and according to
the habitat for fauna. The estimated indices are:
• Shannon Wiener Diversity Index:
𝑯= −∑𝒑𝖎 𝒍𝒏 𝒑𝖎
Where, H = Shannon Wiener diversity index; p = proportion of individuals belonging to the i-
i
th species. This index is a measurement of diversity, expressed from the number of species
(specific richness) and its abundance, thus, the higher the value of H, the greater the diversity of
species (Table 3-11). As in the case of Simpson, the most diverse community is the community with
less dominance. The qualification is given in the following ranges:

• Simpson index
The Simpson index is an index of dominance rather than diversity and represents the probability of
that two individuals chosen at random belong to the same species (Moreno 2001).
𝒔
𝝀 = ∑𝒑𝒊𝟐
𝒊=𝟏
Where, 𝜆 = Simpson index; p = proportion of individuals that belong to the ith species. It is
i
interpreted as the probability of an intraspecific encounter, this means the probability of
that if you take two individuals at random from the community, they are both of the same species. The higher
is this probability, the less diverse the community is.
f) Species rarefaction curve
One of the properties of species richness is that, for a given area, its estimated value
varies asymptotically as the sample size increases, this relationship being illustrated
by the rarefaction (accumulation) curves of species. Among the characteristics of the rarefaction curve
is the fact that, when the sampling effort tends to an arbitrarily large number, the
slope of the species accumulation curve tends to 0. When this occurs, theoretically, you want
say that the total number of species for the studied area has been reached (Jiménez-Valverde & Hortal
2003). For this reason, accumulation curves are frequently used, both to estimate
by rarefaction the total richness of an area under influence, as to evaluate the sufficiency of a
certain sampling effort.
From the information collected in each of the field campaigns, a curve of
rarefaction of species for the area of influence. The objective of carrying out this lies in analyzing and
evaluate the information collected and, therefore, the characterization of the area of influence.
To generate the rarefaction curves, all the data that confirmed the presence
of species, considering each sampling unit (PMF) as a sample. For the preparation and
EstimateS 9.1.0® software was used to model the rarefaction curves (Colwell 2014).
Subsequently, the theoretical estimated richness of the area of influence was calculated, based on the data obtained.
on the ground. For this, a non-parametric estimator that uses incidence data was used.
(presence/absence) for the calculation of the theoretical richness of the area of influence, with the aim of including
those finds of species that do not allow an estimate of the abundance of the individuals
present in the area of influence (e.g. footprints, feces, etc.). In this sense, the estimator used was
Chao 2 (Chao 1984, 1987; Chao & Lee 1992), because it is considered one of the most precise by studies
empirical studies (González et al. 2010, Walther & Moore 2005).
3-25

=====

Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
g) Identification of species of interest and/or sensitive
From the results obtained at the various sampling points, the species of
interest and/or sensitivity present in the area of influence, in accordance with the following:
Any species that is in any conservation category is considered a species of interest.
according to national legislation, with the exception of those in the category “Least Concern” (LC) and “Out of
of danger” (FP), being of particular interest those that are in the threat category and
those species considered natural monuments (D.S. Nº2/2006 of MINAGRI).
A sensitive species is considered to be any species that may or may not be of interest, and that, due to the relationship
existing between the type and location of the project, and the ecological and/or behavioral characteristics, of
mobility and/or displacement of it, may be affected in some way. Below are indicated
some criteria to identify sensitive species:
• Species that uses a narrower spectrum of resources or supports a narrower range of conditions and
They are adapted to very specific habitats (habitat specialist). They are included in this category
amphibians and reptiles mainly.
• Species that have low mobility (amphibians, reptiles, rodents and marsupials) and that therefore
Therefore, when faced with disturbances in their habitat, they will have a low effective capacity for natural escape.
• Species that have high mobility and/or movement with flight (e.g. bats, birds and
mesomammals) and that in the face of the installation and/or operation of certain structures in
specific sectors (valleys, ravines, biological corridors, among others) can be seen
particularly exposed (e.g. transmission lines, wind towers, roads, water reservoirs,
among others).
• Species that presents behavior and/or conduct that makes it more vulnerable to certain
activities (e.g. fox approaching vehicular traffic areas).
• Species that have a recurring seasonal movement between habitats located at different
altitudes, latitudes and longitudes. It can be associated with feeding or reproductive purposes.
(migratory species).
3.1.4.2 Susceptibility to the effects of Climate Change
Regarding the susceptibility to climate change in the area of influence of the Project and its possible
negative synergies, consult the climate risk maps available in the Climate Risk Atlas
(ARCLIM-MMA)1 for the wildlife component and additionally flora and vegetation.
1 https://arclim.mma.gob.cl/
3-26
==================================================================================
PAGE 42
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Among the best known responses to the effects of climate change on biodiversity, are
find the effect on the geographical distribution of species and ecosystems. Through ARCLIM it is possible
access climate change risk maps for these two levels of biodiversity organization
(species and ecosystems) for continental Chile. Current species distribution data are generated
based on data on species occurrences, obtained from the Global Biodiversity Information Facility
(GBIF) and Botanical Information and Ecological Network (BIEN). On the other hand, the distribution data
ecosystem level were generated from the classification of the vegetation floors of Chile, presented
in the proposed bioclimatic and vegetation synopsis of Chile (Luebert and Pliscoff 2019), defining 125
units for continental Chile.
The climate information was extracted from the regional climate model that allows obtaining a set of
climatic variables for two time periods; current period (1980-2010) and future period (2035-
2065). The spatial resolution for continental Chile of the climate variables is 5 km.
To define climate risk at the species level, ARCLIM uses the final database of occurrences,
extracting current and future values of climate parameters
average temperature values and
annual precipitation by species. This methodology is based on the concept of safety margins in
the response of flora and fauna species with records of occurrence in continental Chile, to variations
in climate change based on the characterization of its current tolerance.
To define risk at the ecosystem level, ARCLIM uses the results of the species-first approach
that allows establishing a climate tolerance metric for the species, calculated for each unit
analysis (5 km pixel). With this data, calculate the average risk value per ecosystem and commune,
for both flora and fauna species.
The risk to climate change on the loss of fauna is defined by the following formula
Risk = Threat x Exposure x Vulnerability
The threat corresponds to the difference between the current and future climate (defined for temperature
annual average and annual precipitation). The degree of intervention is defined by the loss of surface
with natural vegetation that the territory has experienced in the last 30 years, where a greater
Loss of vegetation increases the sensitivity of species to climate changes. The
Vulnerability is obtained from the quotient between the sensitivity (safety margin for temperature and
precipitation, added for fauna) and the adaptive capacity of the species. At ARCLIM, the capacity
adaptive is defined by the distribution range of a given fauna species (width of
niche), species with a small distribution range, will indicate a low niche breadth and will be more
sensitive to future changes in climate variables. Risk represents the deviation of the
current climatic margins with respect to the future that could support the set of flora and fauna species
fauna in a 5 km pixel. Finally, ARCLIM groups the risk values into quantiles to obtain
five levels (very low, low, medium, high, very high) to climate change for flora and fauna, by ecosystem
and communes on a national scale.
3-27
==================================================================================
PAGE 43
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
On the other hand, to define change in the probability of presence of the species according to the
future projection of climatic variables, in the case of those species that are not available
on the ARCLIM platform, its distribution models were developed through the tool
Maxent from R software.
Species distribution models show a probability of presence of the species in each
pixel (5x5 km2 cells), with 0 being the minimum value (no probability) and 1 being the maximum value
probability. The Maxent tool estimates this probability of presence from a set of
explanatory variables under historical climatic conditions (1980-2010). In this case, the modeling
carried out using as explanatory variables the average annual evapotranspiration, precipitation
annual cumulative, annual average daily solar insolation, annual average minimum temperature
daily and annual average of the daily maximum temperature. Once verified that the model reproduces
adequately the distribution of the species under historical climatic conditions, the
probability of occurrence of the species under future climate conditions (2035-2065) under the scenario
RCP 8.5 (scenario of intense greenhouse gas emissions).
For the interpretation of species distribution models, it is necessary to keep in mind that these
They do not represent the current or future density of the species, but rather represent the probability that in
a certain pixel there are climatic conditions conducive to the existence of a species. In this
In this sense, the models can give a probability of the presence of a species greater than zero in
sectors where there are no records of its occurrence, to the extent that the climatic conditions
are similar to sectors where the species is present. Considering the above, the results are
expressed qualitatively according to specialist criteria.
3.1.5 Results
3.1.5.1 Sites of interest for fauna
If
recorded two sites of interest for the terrestrial fauna component close to the area of influence of the
Project, corresponding to the Nonguén National Park located approximately 8 km south of the
project and the Hualpén Peninsula Nature Sanctuary, located approximately 13 km in the direction
west of the Project. The Project does not have the potential to affect any of them.
Nonguén National Park was created as a national reserve on December 30, 2009 and then
recategorized as Nonguén National Park, with publication in the Official Gazette on August 31,
2021. This wild area protects important species of fauna, among which mammals such as
the mountain monkey (Dromiciops gliroides), fox (Lycalopex culpaeus) and pudú (Pudu puda). Among the
amphibians, including Darwin's frog (Rhinoderma darwini). Of the variety of birds that can be observed
The most notable are the chuncho (Glacidium nana), the harrier (Geranoaetus polyosoma), the owl (Tyto alba), the
Rayadito (Aphrastura spinicauda) and the chucao (Scelorchilus rubecula). As far as flora is concerned, this
National Park protects the last important remnant of the deciduous forest of Concepción, which
3-28
==================================================================================
PAGE 44
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
It previously covered the Coastal mountain range in the region and was replaced almost entirely by crops
agricultural and forestry plantations. In it you can find species that are rare in this area, such as the raulí
(Nothofagus alpina), the tineo (Weinmannia trichosperma) and the huillipatagua (Citronella mucronata)
(www.conaf.cl/parques/parque-nacional-nonguen/).
This Hualpén Peninsula Nature Sanctuary has cliffs and rocks, which allow the
nesting of species of sea and migratory birds, as well as various
species of mammals, birds, reptiles, amphibians and fish. Within the fauna described for this area
protected areas include: mountain monkey (Dromiciops gliroides), red bat (Lasiurus varius),
ashy bat (Lasiurus cinereus), common bat (Tadarida brasiliensis), chilla fox
(Lycalopex griseus), olive mouse (Abrothrix olivaceus), nutria (Myocastor coypus), long-tailed mouse
(Oligoryzomys longicaudatus), yeco (Phalacrocorax brasilianus), cuca heron (Ardea cocoi), great heron
(Ardea alba), little heron (Egretha thula), ring-necked duck (Anas bahemensis), black-necked swan
(Cygnus melancoryphus), black-headed jack (Coragyps atratus), red-headed jack (Cathartes
aura), short-tailed snake (Tachymenis chilensis), long-tailed snake (Philodryas chamissonis), lizard
chilean (Liolaemus chilensis), slender lizard (Liolaemus tenuis), masked frog (Batrachyla taeniata),
roseate leaf litter frog (Eupsophus roseus), four-eyed toad (Pleurodema thaul) and Chilean frog
(Calyptocephalella gayi). This Sanctuary is a highly fragile lake and swamp ecosystem, which
presents species with conservation problems such as the black-necked swan and the swamp crow,
especially due to the pressure exerted by the extraction of reeds, hunting and poaching (Council of
Natural Monuments, seen: www.monumentos.gob.cl/monumentos/santuarios-de-la-
nature/peninsula-hualpen).
3.1.5.2 Native fauna
i. Potential Native Fauna
From the bibliographic review, 124 potential species of native terrestrial fauna were identified.
described for the area of influence, which correspond to: 5 amphibians, 8 reptiles, 90 birds and 21 mammals.
Of the identified species, 47 (37.9% of the total) are cataloged in some category of
conservation according to current national legislation. These correspond to 5 amphibians, 8 reptiles, 16 birds and
18 mammals. Of these, 8 are in the threat category (Endangered and Vulnerable) and correspond to 3
amphibians, 1 reptile, 2 birds and 2 mammals.
In relation to endemism, 12 species of terrestrial vertebrates were identified with distribution
exclusive in our country: 3 amphibians, 3 reptiles, 5 birds and 1 mammal, which corresponds to 9.67% of the
species described for the area.
Regarding the degree of mobility that the species present, 19 are of low mobility (five
or amphibians, eight
reptiles and six small mammals) and 105 highly mobile (90 birds and 15 mammals). Regarding the status

Birds
Based on the sampling effort of the campaign carried out, the presence of 37 species of birds was recorded
in the area of influence. Of the total species, four of them present some conservation category
based on current national legislation, none in the threat category.
All species in this group are highly mobile. Regarding migratory status, 28 species are
residents and nine species migrate within the national territory. In relation to the BSE criteria,
They register 28 species cataloged as beneficial for forestry and agricultural activity (B), six species
cataloged with reduced population densities (S) and 15 species are classified as beneficial
for the maintenance of the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
In particular with respect to records by environment, the following is indicated:
• In the forest environment, the presence of 15 species was recorded, highlighting the presence of the
Dominican gull (L. dominicanus), a species that has an estimated density of 42.87
ind/ha and a relative abundance of 73.72% with respect to the total number of individuals. The species follows
small hummingbird (S. sephaniodes). The rest of the species present densities less than 2 ind/ha and
a representativeness of less than 3% (Figure 3-13). In addition, the presence of the chuncho (G.
nana), concón (S. rufipex) and blind hen (S. longirostris), using the playback methodology of
nocturnal birds of prey, this methodology does not provide information about the number of
individuals, so the calculation of density and abundance parameters is not possible and only
considered for species richness.
• In the scrub environment, 18 species were observed, highlighting the presence of the seagull.
dominicana (L. dominicanus), a species that has an estimated density of 141.65 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 73.19% with respect to the total number of individuals. It is followed by the head jote species
black (C. atratus). The rest of the species present densities less than 3 ind/ha and a
representativeness less than 4% (Figure 3-14).
• In the modified environment, the presence of 21 species was recorded, once again dominating the
Dominican gull species (L. dominicanus), which has a density of 34.03 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 15.22%. It is followed by the black-headed jote species (C. atratus) with 13.39
ind/ha and a relative abundance of 6.21% of the total number of specimens for this environment. The rest of
The species present densities below 10 ind/ha and representativeness less than 5% (Figure 3-15).
• The plantation environment was the one that recorded the greatest richness, reaching 27 species. between
Among them, the Dominican gull (L. dominicanus) stands out again, with a density of 51.16
ind/ha and a relative abundance of 23.17%. It is followed by the black-headed jote species (C. atratus)
with a density of 19.44 ind/ha and a relative abundance of 8.81% of the total number of specimens for
this environment. The rest of the species present densities below 5 ind/ha and representativeness
less than 2% (Figure 3-16 and Figure 3-17). In addition, the presence of the owl species (T.
alba) through the playback methodology of nocturnal birds of prey.

threat.

migratory, four species are resident and one migratory (Tadarida brasiliensis). In relation to the

Reptilia
From the collection of information in the field, four species of reptiles were identified,
ran
spondents to the Schröeder's lizard (Liolaemus schroederi), the slender lizard (Liolaemus
tenuis), the lemniscata lizard (Liolaemus lemniscatus) and the painted lizard (Liolaemus pictus). All the
The aforementioned species are cataloged in some national conservation category,
according to current national legislation; and one of them (Liolaemus schroederi, “Vulnerable”), presents
threat category.
Regarding origin, one is endemic (L. schroederi) to the country and three of native origin. All species
registered are of low mobility and residents in the national territory. Regarding the BSE criteria, three
of the observed species are cataloged with reduced population densities (S) and all are beneficial
for the maintenance of the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
In particular with respect to records by environment, the following is indicated:
• In the forest environment, the presence of the slender lizard (L. tenuis) was recorded, with a density
of 4.17 ind/ha and a relative abundance of 5% of the total number of specimens for this environment.
• In the scrub environment the presence of two species was recorded, highlighting the presence of the
slender lizard (L. tenuis), with an estimated density of 9.38 ind/ha and a relative abundance
of 0.56% of the total number of specimens registered for this environment, followed by the lizard
lemniscata (L. lemniscatus) with an estimated density of 1.04 ind/ha and a relative abundance
of 0.06% of the total number of individuals registered for this environment (Figure 3-22).
• In the modified environment, the presence of two species was recorded, highlighting the presence of the
slender lizard (L. tenuis), with an estimated density of 5.00 ind/ha and a relative abundance
of 1.27% of the total number of specimens registered for this environment, followed by the lizard
lemniscata (L. lemniscatus) with an estimated density of 1.67 ind/ha and a relative abundance
of 0.42% of the total number of individuals registered for this environment (Figure 3-23).
• In the plantation environment, the presence of four species was recorded; the slender lizard (L. tenuis)
with the highest estimated density of 2.45 ind/ha and a relative abundance of 2.55% of the total
of the individuals recorded for this environment, followed by Schröeder's lizard (L.
schroederi), the lemniscata lizard (L. lemniscatus) and the painted lizard (L. pictus), all with a
estimated density of 0.25 ind/ha and a relative abundance of 0.26% of the total number of specimens
sighted in this environment (Figure 3-24 and Figure 3-25).

Birds
Based on the sampling effort of the campaign carried out, the presence of 33 species of birds was recorded
in the area of influence. Of the total number of species, three of them present some conservation category in
based on current national legislation, none in the threat category.
All species in this group are highly mobile. Regarding migratory status, 26 species are
residents and seven species migrate within the national territory. In relation to the BSE criteria,
register 26 species cataloged as beneficial for forestry and agricultural activity (B), four
species cataloged with reduced population densities (S) and 13 species are cataloged as
beneficial for maintaining the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
In particular with respect to records by environment, the following is indicated:
• In the forest environment, the presence of 19 species was recorded, highlighting the presence of the
Dominican gull (L. dominicanus) and the lesser hummingbird (S. sephaniodes), both have a
estimated density of 3.18 ind/ha and a relative abundance of 12.50% with respect to the total
individuals. They are followed by the red-headed jote species (C. aura). The rest of the species present
densities less than 2 ind/ha and a representativeness less than 8% (Figure 3-26). Furthermore, it
recorded the presence of the chuncho (G. nana) and concón (S. rufipex) using the methodology of
playback of nocturnal birds of prey.
• In the scrub environment, 23 species were observed, highlighting the presence of the seagull.
dominicana (L. dominicanus), a species that has an estimated density of 342.18 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 66.48% with respect to the total number of individuals. It is followed by the head jote species
black (C. atratus). The rest of the species present densities less than 10 ind/ha and a
representativeness less than 2% (Figure 3-27).
• In the modified environment, the presence of 25 species was recorded, once again dominating the
Dominican gull species (L. dominicanus), which has a density of 79.49 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 65.82%. It is followed by the black-headed jote species (C. atratus) with 7.90
ind/ha and a relative abundance of 6.54% of the total number of specimens for this environment. The rest of
The species present densities below 7 ind/ha and representativeness less than 6% (Figure 3-28).
• The plantation environment was the one that recorded the greatest richness, reaching 30 species. between
Among them, the Dominican gull (L. dominicanus) stands out again, with a density of 5.92
ind/ha and a relative abundance of 20.15%. It is followed by the species small hummingbird (S. sephaniodes)
with 4.05 ind/ha and a relative abundance of 13.78% of the total number of specimens for this environment.
The rest of the species present densities below 3 ind/ha and representation less than 8%.
(Figure 3-29). In addition, the presence of concón (S. rufipex) was recorded using the
playback of nocturnal birds of prey.

This methodology does not provide information on the number of individuals, so it was not possible

Birds
Based on the sampling effort of the campaign carried out, the presence of 38 species of birds was recorded
in the area of influence. Of the total species, four present some conservation category, different
to the threat categories, based on current national legislation.

All species in this group are highly mobile. Regarding migratory status, 25 species are
residents, 12 species migrate within the national territory and one outside the national territory. In relation
With the BSE criteria, 28 species cataloged as beneficial for the activity are registered
silvoagropecuaria (B), five species cataloged with reduced population densities (S) and 17 species
They are classified as beneficial for maintaining the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
In particular with respect to records by environment, the following is indicated:
• In the forest environment, the presence of 12 species was recorded, highlighting the presence of the
Dominican gull (L. dominicanus), with an estimated density of 12.73 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 46.88% with respect to the total number of individuals. They are followed by the chincol species (Z.
capensis). The rest of the species present densities less than 2 ind/ha and a
representativeness
or 7% (Figure 3-37). In addition, the presence of concón (S.
rufipex) and blind hen (S. longirostris) using the raptor playback methodology
nocturnal.
• In the scrub environment, 22 species were observed, highlighting the presence of the seagull.
dominicana (L. dominicanus), a species that has an estimated density of 968.93 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 94.07% with respect to the total number of individuals. It is followed by the head jote species
black (C. atratus). The rest of the species present densities less than 4 ind/ha and a
representativeness less than 1% (Figure 3-38).
• In the modified environment, the presence of 23 species was recorded, once again dominating the
Dominican gull species (L. dominicanus), which has a density of 749.31 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 91.36%. It is followed by the black-headed jote species (C. atratus) with 13.89
ind/ha and a relative abundance of 1.69% of the total number of specimens for this environment. The rest of
The species present densities below 10 ind/ha and representativeness less than 1.5% (Figure
3-39).
• The plantation environment was the one that recorded the greatest richness, reaching 35 species. between
Among them, the Dominican gull (L. dominicanus) stands out again, with a density of 4.95
ind/ha and a relative abundance of 14.83%. They are followed by the species pintadito (A. spinicauda) and
small hummingbird (S. sephaniodes) with 2.34 ind/ha and a relative abundance of 6.99% of the total
exemplars for this environment. The rest of the species present densities below 2 ind/ha and
representativeness less than 6% (Figure 3-39 and Figure 3-40). Furthermore, the presence of the
chuncho (G. nana), concón (S. rufipex) and blind hen (S. longirostris) using the methodology of
playback of nocturnal birds of prey.

Mammalia
From the collection of information in the field, seven species of mammals were identified in the
area of influence. Of the registered species, four are cataloged in some category of
conservation based on current Chilean environmental legislation, but none in the threat category.
Four species have low mobility (all rodents) and three have high mobility. Regarding the status
migratory, six species are resident and one migratory (Tadarida brasiliensis). In relation to the
BSE criteria, three species are classified as beneficial for the activity
silvoagropecuaria (B) and four as beneficial species for maintaining the balance of ecosystems
natural (E).
In particular with respect to records by environment, the following is indicated:
• In the forest environment, the presence of three species was confirmed, highlighting the presence of
olive mouse (A. olivaceus), a species that has a density of 7.11 ind/ha and an abundance
relative of 6.25% with respect to the total species associated with this environment, followed by the mouse
longhair (A. longipilis) with a density of 3.56 ind/ha and a relative abundance of 3.13%
of the total number of specimens for this environment (Figure 3-42). Furthermore, the presence of the
southern mouse-eared bat (M. chiloensis) using an ultrasound detector, this
methodology does not provide information on the number of individuals, so it was not possible to
calculation of density or abundance, they were only considered as species richness (Figure 3-43).
• In the scrub environment, the presence of quique (G. cuja) was recorded using trap records.
camera (Figure 3-43).
• In the plantation environment, the presence of five species was recorded, highlighting the presence of
olive mouse (A. olivaceus), a species that has a density of 14.00 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 13.35% with respect to the total species associated with this environment,
followed by the long-haired mouse (A. longipilis), with a density of 5.33 ind/ha and an abundance
3-58
==================================================================================
PAGE 74
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
relative of 5.08% (Figure 3-43). In addition, the presence of the common bat (T.
brasiliensis), using an ultrasound detector (Figure 3-45).
• Despite the sampling effort, no mammals were recorded in the modified environment.

d) Campaign 4 (summer 2023)
In relation to the records by environment of the summer campaign, the following is indicated:
• In the forest environment, 26 species were recorded, corresponding to three reptiles, 20 species of
birds and three mammals. In the scrub environment, 24 species were recorded, four species of
reptiles and 20 birds. In the modified environment, 22 species were observed, four reptiles and 18
birds. Finally, the richest environment corresponds to plantations, with 50 species,
distributed in one amphibian, five reptiles, 36 birds and eight mammals.
Amphibia
Based on the sampling effort of the campaign carried out, the presence of a species of
amphibian, corresponding to the four-eyed toad (Pleuroderma thaul). This species is found
classified as “Near Threatened” according to current national legislation, so it does not present a category
of threat.
It is a species of low mobility, of native origin and resident in the national territory. As for the
BSE criteria, it is a species classified as beneficial for maintaining the balance of
natural ecosystems (E).
• In the plantation environment, the presence of the aforementioned species (P. thaul) was recorded with
an estimated density of 0.23 ind/ha and a relative abundance of 0.09% of the total
specimens registered for this environment.

Reptilia
From the collection of information in the field, five species of reptiles were identified,
corresponding to Schröeder's lizard (Liolaemus schroederi), blue-bellied lizard (Liolaemus
cyanogaster), the slender lizard (Liolaemus tenuis), the lemniscata lizard (Liolaemus lemniscatus) and the
Chilean lizard (Liolaemus chiliensis) (Figure 3-46). All the aforementioned species are found
cataloged in some national conservation category, according to current national legislation; and one of
They (Liolaemus schroederi, “Vulnerable”), present a threat category.
Regarding origin, one is endemic (L. schroederi) to the country and four of native origin. All species
registered are of low mobility and residents in the national territory. Regarding the BSE criteria, a
species is classified as beneficial for forestry and agricultural activity (B), three of the species
observed are classified as having reduced population densities (S) and all of them are beneficial for the
maintenance of the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
In particular with respect to records by environment, the following is indicated:
• In the forest environment, the presence of three species was observed, highlighting the presence of the
slender lizard (L. tenuis), with an estimated density of 8.33 ind/ha and a relative abundance
of 3.49% of the total specimens registered for this environment, followed by the lizard
lemniscata (L. lemniscatus) and then the Chilean lizard (L. chiliensis) (F
Figure 3-47).
• In the scrub environment the presence of four species was recorded, highlighting the presence of the
slender lizard (L. tenuis), with an estimated density of 11.46 ind/ha and a relative abundance
of 0.76% of the total number of specimens registered for this environment, followed by the lizard
lemniscata (L. lemniscatus), with an estimated density of 8.33 ind/ha and a relative abundance
of 0.55% of the total number of individuals registered for this environment (Figure 3-48).
• In the modified environment, the presence of four species was recorded, highlighting the presence
of the Chilean lizard (L. chiliensis), with an estimated density of 7.64 ind/ha and an abundance
relative of 1.57% of the total specimens registered for this environment, followed by the lizard
slender (L. tenuis), with an estimated density of 6.25 ind/ha and a relative abundance of 1.28%
of the total number of individuals registered for this environment (Figure 3-49).
• In the plantation environment, the presence of five species was recorded; the slender lizard (L. tenuis)
with the highest estimated density of 4.17 ind/ha and a relative abundance of 1.57% of the total
of the individuals recorded for this environment, followed by the Chilean lizard (L. chiliensis), with
3-62

Rare Earths
an estimated density of 2.78 ind/ha and a relative abundance of 1.05% of the total number of specimens
sighted in this environment (Figure 3-50).

in the area of influence. Of the total species, six present some conservation category, other than

All species in this group are highly mobile. Regarding migratory status, 29 species are
residents, 11 species migrate within the national territory and two outside the national territory. In relation
With the BSE criteria, 30 species cataloged as beneficial for the activity are registered
silvoagropecuaria (B), seven species cataloged with reduced population densities (S) and 16 species
They are classified as beneficial for maintaining the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
In particular with respect to records by environment, the following is indicated:
• In the forest environment, the presence of 20 species was recorded, highlighting the presence of the
Dominican gull (L. dominicanus), with an estimated density of 23.77 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 32.56% with respect to the total number of individuals. They are followed by the head jote species
red (C. aura). The rest of the species present densities less than 8 ind/ha and a
representativeness less than 11% (Figure 3-51). In addition, the presence of chuncho (G.
nana), concón (S. rufipex), blind hen (S. longirostris) and owl (T. alba), through the
playback methodology of nocturnal birds of prey.
• In the scrub environment, 20 species were observed, highlighting the presence of the seagull.
dominicana (L. dominicanus), a species that has an estimated density of 367.5 ind/ha and a
Relative abundance of 79.82% with respect to the total number of individuals. It is followed by the head jote species
black (C. atratus) and then the red-headed jote (C. aura). The rest of the species present
densities less than 4 ind/ha and a representativeness less than 1% (Figure 3-52).
• In the modified environment, the presence of 18 species was recorded, once again dominating the
Dominican gull species (L. dominicanus), which has a density of 77.92 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 52.28%. It is followed by the species black-headed jote (C. atratus) and then the jote
red-headed (C. aura). The rest of the species present densities below 8 ind/ha and
representativeness less than 6% (Figure 3-53).
• The plantation environment was the one that recorded the greatest richness, reaching 36 species. between
Among them, the Dominican gull (L. dominicanus) stands out again, with a density of 42.89
ind/ha and a relative abundance of 53.02%. They are followed by the black-headed jote species (C.
atratus) and fío fío (E. albiceps) with 6.37 and 6.09 ind/ha respectively, and a relative abundance
of 7.87 and 7.52% of the total number of specimens for this environment. The rest of the species present
densities below 4 ind/ha and representativeness less than 4% (Figure 3-54 and Figure 3-55). Furthermore,
The presence of concón (S. rufipex) and blind hen (S. longirostris) was recorded by means of
playback methodology of nocturnal birds of prey.

Mammalia
From the collection of information in the field, nine species of mammals were identified in the
area of influence. Of the registered species, six are cataloged in some category of
conservation based on current Chilean environmental legislation, but none in the threat category.
Five species have low mobility (all rodents) and four have high mobility. Regarding the status
migratory, seven species are resident and one migratory (Lasiurus varius). In relation to the criteria
BSE, three species are classified as beneficial for forestry and agricultural activity (B),
one species cataloged with reduced population densities (S) and four as beneficial species for
the maintenance of the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
In particular with respect to records by environment, the following is indicated:
• In the forest environment, the presence of three species was confirmed, highlighting the presence of
long-haired mouse (A. longipilis), a species that has a density of 3.56 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 1.16% with respect to the total species associated with this environment, followed
of the olive mouse (A. olivaceus) with a density of 1.78 ind/ha and a relative abundance of
0.58% of the total number of specimens for this environment (Figure 3-56). Furthermore, the presence was recorded
of the red bat (L. varius) using an ultrasound detector, this methodology does not provide
information on the number of individuals so it was not possible to calculate density or
abundance, were only considered as species richness (Figure 3-56 and Figure 3-57).
• In the plantation environment, the presence of eight species was recorded, highlighting the presence of
olive mouse (A. olivaceus), a species that has a density of 7.56 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 2.97% with respect to the total species associated with this environment, followed
of the long-haired mouse (A. longipilis), with a density of 4.89 ind/ha and a relative abundance of
1.92% (Figure 3-57). In addition, the presence of the mountain monkey (D. gliroides) and the fox was recorded.
culpeo (L. culpaeus), through camera trap records; and the southern long-eared bat (H.

e) Campaign 5 (spring 2023)
In relation to the records by environment of the spring campaign, the following is indicated:
In the forest environment, 22 species were recorded, corresponding to five reptiles, 15 species of birds
and two of mammals. In the scrub environment, 27 species, three reptiles and 24 birds, were recorded. In the
modified environment, 28 species were observed, five reptiles and 23 birds. Finally, the environment of greater
richness corresponds to plantation, with 41 species, distributed in five reptiles, 32 birds and four
mammals.
Amphibia
Despite the sampling effort of the campaign carried out, the presence of specimens of
amphibians.
Reptilia
From the collection of information in the field, six species of reptiles were identified,
corresponding to Schröeder's lizard (Liolaemus schroederi), blue-bellied lizard (Liolaemus
cyanogaster), painted lizard (Liolaemus pictus), slender lizard (Liolaemus tenuis), lemniscata lizard
(Liolaemus lemniscatus) and the Chilean lizard (Liolaemus chiliensis) (Figure 3-60). All species before
mentioned are cataloged in some national conservation category, according to legislation
current national and one of them (Liolaemus schroederi, “Vulnerable”), has a threat category.
Regarding origin, one is endemic to the country (L. schroederi) and five of native origin. All species
registered are of low mobility and residents in the national territory. Regarding the BSE criteria, a
species is classified as beneficial for forestry and agricultural activity (B), three of the species
observed are classified as having reduced population densities (S) and all of them are beneficial for the
maintenance of the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
In particular with respect to records by environment, the following is indicated:

• In the forest environment, the presence of five species was observed, highlighting the presence of the
slender lizard (L. tenuis), with an estimated density of 52.08 ind/ha and a relative abundance
of 2.77% of the total number of specimens registered for this environment, followed by. chilean lizard
(L. chiliensis) with an estimated density of 6.25 ind/ha and a relative abundance of 0.33% of the
total number of individuals recorded for this environment (Figure 3-61).
• In the scrub environment the presence of four species was recorded, highlighting the presence of the
slender lizard (L. tenuis), with an estimated density of 23.96 ind/ha and a relative abundance
of 0.62% of the total number of specimens registered for this environment, followed by the lizard
lemniscata (L. lemniscatus) with an estimated density of 2.08 ind/ha and a relative abundance
of 0.05% of the total number of individuals registered for this environment (Figure 3-62).
• In the modified environment, the presence of five species was recorded, highlighting the presence of
the slender lizard (L. tenuis), with an estimated density of 11.11 ind/ha and an abundance
relative of 0.76% of the total specimens registered for this environment, followed by the lizard
Chilean (L. chiliensis), with an estimated density of 2.78 ind/ha and a relative abundance of
0.19% of the total number of individuals registered for this environment (Figure 3-63).
• In the plantation environment, the presence of five species was also recorded, highlighting the
presence of the slender lizard (L. tenuis) with the highest estimated density of 11.57 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 3.05% of the total individuals recorded for this environment,
followed by the blue-bellied lizard (L. cyanogaster), with an estimated density of 2.31 ind/ha
and a relative abundance of 0.61% of the total number of specimens seen in this environment (Figure
3-64).

Birds
Based on the sampling effort of the campaign carried out, the presence of 37 species of birds was recorded
in the area of influence. Of the total species, five present some conservation category, different
to the threat categories, based on current national legislation.
All species in this group are highly mobile. Regarding migratory status, 26 species are
residents, 10 species migrate within the national territory and one outside the national territory. In relation
With the BSE criteria, 27 species cataloged as beneficial for the activity are registered
silvoagropecuaria (B), six species cataloged with reduced population densities (S) and 15 species
They are classified as beneficial for maintaining the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
In particular with respect to records by environment, the following is indicated:
• In the forest environment, the presence of 15 species was recorded, highlighting the presence of the
Dominican gull (L. dominicanus), with an estimated density of 457.32 ind/ha and a
Relative abundance of 79.42% with respect to the total number of individuals. It is followed by the head jote species
red (C. aura). The rest of the species present densities less than 16 ind/ha and a
representativeness less than 3% (Figure 3-65).
• In the scrub environment, 24 species were observed, highlighting the presence of the seagull.
dominicana (L. dominicanus), a species that has an estimated density of 800.96 ind/ha and a
relative abundance of 67.37% with respect to the total number of individuals. They are followed by the jote species of
black-headed (C. atratus) and red-headed jote. The rest of the species have densities
less than 8 ind/ha and a representativeness less than 1% (Figure 3-66).
• In the modified environment, the presence of 23 species was recorded, once again dominating the
Dominican gull species (L. dominicanus), which has a density of 257.96 ind/ha and a

• The plantation environment was the one that recorded the greatest richness, reaching 33 species. between

iii. Introduced fauna
In the area of influence, for the five sampling campaigns, seven species of origin were recorded
introduced; two birds and five mammals. Of these, four are considered harmful according to Article 6.
of the Hunting Law (Table 3-14 and Figure 3-72).

a) Diversity indices
The indices indicate that, comparing the four types of environments in the area of influence, during the five
ground campaigns. In autumn 2022, the environment with the greatest diversity corresponds to modified
(H'=2.49), in winter 2022 to forest (H'=0.93), in spring 2022 to plantation (H'=0.93), in summer 2023
to forest (H'=0.85), and in spring 2023 to plantation (H'=2.09).
On the other hand, and
According to the diversity provided by the Simpson index (D), it is possible to infer
that in autumn 2022 the environment with the greatest diversity corresponds to modified (D=0.87), in winter
2022 to scrub (D=0.51), in spring 2022 to scrub (D=0.89), in summer 2023 to scrub (D=0.65), and
in spring 2023 to plantation (D=0.71). Considering that this index indicates that an environment is more
diverse in values close to one, it can be seen that the values of greatest diversity are observed in
the plantation environment during spring 2023 (Table 3-15).

3.1.5.3 Species rarefaction curves
The sufficiency of the sampling effort was analyzed based on the results obtained, through the
construction of the species rarefaction curve based on the Chao2 estimator as indicated in the
methodology.
Figure 3-73 shows the rarefaction curve generated based on the information collected for the five
campaigns, considering each fauna sampling point (PMF) as a sample. Of this,
shows that 85% of the total estimated (theoretical) wealth was recorded in the area of influence, with
an observed richness of 73 of a total of 86 species, according to the Chao2 estimator. According to Jiménez-Valverde
& Hortal (2003), recording from 70% of the estimated theoretical wealth onwards would show a
sufficiency in sampling, considering this a representative sampling of the area of influence.

3.1.5.4 Species of interest and/or sensitive in the project area
Of the total species recorded during the five sampling campaigns and field visits
complementary, 40 have been considered species of interest and/or sensitive. Of these, 12
correspond to species of interest (two amphibians, one reptile, two birds and six mammals) and 37 are species
sensitive (two amphibians, six reptiles, 18 birds and 11 mammals). Below are the species that
are included within these categories.

specific construction and operation characteristics. Below is the details of the species
sensitive registered for each class:
• In relation to amphibians, there is the roseate leaf litter frog (E. roseus) and the toad

mobility, which is summarized in a low effective natural escape capacity in the face of disturbances
in their habitats.
• In the reptile group, all registered species are considered (L. chiliensis, L. cyanogaster,
L. lemniscatus, L. pictus, L. schroederi, L. tenuis), due to their low mobility and therefore a low
effective capacity for natural escape from disturbances in their habitats.

i. Description of the registered species of interest and/or sensitive species
Below, a description is indicated for each species of interest and/or sensitive, based on the
bibliography available for each one, which allows weighing the relevance of the findings made.
a) Eupsophus roseus
The roseate leaf litter frog (Eupsophus roseus) is a species of amphibian belonging to the family
Cycloramphidae. Eupsophus large, robust, with a round and wide head, with a circular tympanum
3-86

==================================================================================

Rare Earths
evident. Thick legs with free toes and smooth skin. There is great controversy about its taxonomy and
distribution, being bibliographically distributed from Cautín (Araucanía region) to Valdivia
(Los Ríos region) (Rabanal and Nuñez 2008). It is mainly terrestrial and inhabits forest sectors
Valdivian Nothofagus type with forest associations of the oak-raulí-coihue type (Rabanal and Nuñez 2008).
It is classified as “Vulnerable” according to the D.S. N°41/2011 that formalizes the sixth process of
classification of the RCE. According to the IUCN (International Union for the Conservation of Nature) classification
Nature), its category is "Least Concern" (IUCN SSC Amphibian Specialist Group 2023).
b) Pleurodema thaul
The four-eyed toad (P. thaul) is a species of native amphibian belonging to the family Leiuperidae.
Very abundant species and wide distribution, from the Atacama region (27° S) to the Aysén region (46°
S), from sea level to 2,100 m.a.s.l. (Charrier 2019). Also present in Argentina. The
population of Robinson Crusoe Island is introduced, as would also be the one reported in the city of
Antofagasta (Lobos et al. 2010). It is mainly terrestrial and inhabits all types of environments, both
pristine forest sectors as well as sectors bordering urban areas. Forms large aggregations
reproductive on the banks of estuaries and streams, where amplexus, oviposition occurs (in clusters)
and larval development. They feed mainly on arthropods, arachnids and diptera (Lobos et al.
2010, Charrier 2019). Drought, forest fires, drainage have been described as threat factors.
of wetlands for urbanization and contamination by agrochemicals (Charrier 2019).
It is classified as “Almost Threatened” according to the D.S. N°41/2011 that makes the sixth process official
classification of the FTR. At a global level it is classified as “Least Concern” (IUCN SSC Amphibian
Specialist Group 2015). Regarding the BSE criteria, it is considered a beneficial species for the
maintenance of the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
c) Liolaemus chiliensis
The Chilean lizard (Liolaemus chiliensis) is a species of large lizard that exceeds the
Cloacal snout length of 80-100 mm, the male has two precloacal pores. It has 27 to 32 scales
around the middle of the body. They present sexual dimorphism, the male being greenish brown,
while the female is olive green. It lives in areas of scrubland and forests (scrublands
xerophytic and sclerophytic), sometimes with climbing habits. Its diet is insectivorous. The
Current and potential threats are related to the loss of habitats due to their transformation,
mainly on agricultural and secondarily forestry lands (Pincheira & Nuñez 2005, Mella
2017).
At the local level it is classified as “Least Concern” according to the D.S. N°19/2012 of the MMA,
same category presented at a global level by the IUCN (Ávila et al. 2016a).

Rare Earths
d) Liolaemus cyanogaster
The blue-bellied lizard (Liolaemus cyanogaster) is a species of reptile in the family Liolamidae.
considered a native of the country. Its distribution goes from the Maule region to the region of the
lakes,
between 0 and 1,200 meters above sea level. It lives mainly on logs, bushes and on the ground, species
climber Of insectivorous habits and viviparous reproduction (Demangel, 2016; Mella, 2017).
At the local level it is classified as “Least Concern” according to the D.S. N°23/2019 of the MMA.
According to the IUCN classification (Ávila et al. 2016b), it also has the “Least Concern” category.
e) Liolaemus lemniscatus
The lemniscata lizard (Liolaemus lemniscatus) is a species of reptile belonging to the order Squamata.
and family Liolamidae, is widely distributed in Chile, from the Coquimbo region (northern
Ovalle), to the Los Ríos region (north of Valdivia), and from the Andes Mountains (2,100 m.a.s.l.)
to the coast (Pincheira & Nuñez 2005; Mella, 2017). It is one of the most common lizards in the territory
Chilean. It inhabits both natural environments and those that have been intervened (e.g. agricultural areas), it is
markedly terrestrial, it hides in the grasslands, sometimes in the trees, but a few centimeters from the
ground. It feeds mainly on insects (Pincheira & Nuñez 2005).
At a global level it is classified as “Least Concern” (Avilés 2016), at a local level it is
It is in the Least Concern category, according to the eighth process of the RCE (D.S. N°19/2013 MMA).
f) Liolaemus pictus
The painted lizard (Liolaemus pictus) is a species of reptile native to Chile in the order Squamata and the
family Tropiduridae. It is found from the north of the Biobío region to the Aysén region, between 0
and 1,700 meters above sea level. It is medium in size and the tail reaches a little more than one and a half times the length of the
body. Head longer than wide with pointed snout. The neck is folded and has scales
granular and juxtaposed. The dorsal scales are rounded, small and slightly quilled and
subricate to imbricate. Only males have three precloacal pores. Presents a pattern
composed of clear supraocular bands, accompanied by triangular melanic spots
zigzags, which also cover the occipital band. The coloration is very variable; there are
specimens of dark brown or olive brown tones and others with yellow, orange, red, green scales
and/or blues; the most colorful specimens are towards the south. It is omnivorous, in some populations
The consumption of fruits predominates, while others feed exclusively on invertebrates such as
such as diptera, hemiptera and spiders (Demangel 2016).
At a global level it is classified according to IUCN as “Least Concern” (Abdala et al. 2016a). In Chile to
local level according to RCE it is classified as “Least Concern (LC)” (D.S. N°19/2012 MMA).

g) Liolaemus schroederi
Schröeder's lizard (Liolaemus schroederi) is a species of reptile in the family Liolamidae.
considered endemic to the country. Its distribution goes from the Metropolitan region to the region of
Araucanía, between 200 and 2,600 meters above sea level. It lives mainly in forests and bushes, very shy.
to human presence. Of insectivorous habits and viviparous reproduction (Demangel 2016, Mella 2017).
At the local level it is classified as “Vulnerable” according to D.S. N°16/2016 of the Ministry of the Environment
Environment. According to the IUCN classification, it does not present a threat category as it is classified as
“Least concern” (Avilés 2016b).
h) Liolaemus tenuis
The slender lizard (Liolaemus tenuis) is a species of reptile in the family Liolamidae, considered to be
native to the national territory. Its distribution goes from the Coquimbo region to Los Lagos, between 0 and
1,800 meters m.a.s.l. It mainly inhabits forests, bushes and large rocks. Also common in
urban parks and human settlements. Of insectivorous habits (coleopterans and diptera) and of
oviparous reproduction. It is a very territorial species (Demangel 2016, Mella 2017).
At the local level it is classified as “Least Concern” according to D.S. N°19/2012 of the Ministry
of the Environment. According to the IUCN classification (Abdala et al. 2016a), it has category
“Least concern.”
i) Accipiter bicolor
The peuquito (Accipiter bicolor), and
It is a species of bird of prey belonging to the order Accipitriformes and the
family Accipitridae, described by Philippi. It has a wide neotropical geographical distribution but
discontinuous. Its presence extends to both sides of the Andes Mountains in the center and south of
Chile and southwestern Argentina. In Chile, its range covers from the Coquimbo Region to the
Magallanes, with records of sightings even in urban areas such as Santiago (Medrano et al. 2018).
Regarding its appearance, the adult exhibits a characteristic "bicolor" pattern on the back and ventral. The
plumage on the back is dark gray, while the ventral area shows an ashy hue
pale and rufous with transverse light gray lines. In addition, it has reddish breeches and undercover feathers.
white tails. Its legs and toes are long and yellow, with a long tail and short wings.
rounded (Alvarado et al. 2015).
At the local level it is classified as “Least Concern” according to D.S. N°16/2020 of the Ministry
of the Environment. According to the IUCN classification (BirdLife International 2020a), it has category
“Least concern.”
