j) Caracara plancus
The common carapace or traro (Caracara plancus) is a native bird of the country, belonging to the order
Falconiforme and the family Falconidae. In Chile it lives from the region of Arica and Parinacota to Tierra del
Fire in the Magallanes region, being more abundant in the southern and extreme southern areas. It is distributed
from sea level to 2,000 meters above sea level, occupying open fields, scrubland areas and forests.
Their diet is carnivorous and scavenger; Their way of feeding is far from the methods used by the majority
of the birds of prey that hunt in flight, as it prefers to hunt on the ground, cornering its prey until it reaches it
and, on other occasions, it can be found along with other scavengers feeding on an animal already
dead. The reproduction period runs from September to October in the north-central zone and from November
to December in the southern zone, nesting preferably in taller trees, but it also nests
They record in lower trees such as thorns or bushes. She normally lays two eggs in each clutch.
of a creamy pink color with dark red or brick spots.
Its international conservation status is “Least Concern” according to IUCN (BirdLife International).
2023). In Chile it is not classified according to the criteria of the Hunting Law or RCE, so it does not present
conservation category according to current national regulations.
k) Cathartes aura
The red-headed jote (Cathartes aura) is a species of bird belonging to the order Cathartiformes.
and family Cathartidae. It is distributed from Arica to Magallanes, from sea level to 2,500
m.s.n.m. It feeds on carrion and can rarely kill its prey. It reproduces between
months of September and October, with a lay of 1-2 white eggs with red spots.
At a global level it is considered in the “Least Concern” category (BirdLife International 2018a), in Chile
It is not classified according to the criteria of the Hunting Law or RCE.
l) Colaptes pitius
The pitío (Colaptes pitius) is a species of bird belonging to the order Piciformes and family Picidae. In Chile
It is distributed from Coquimbo to Magallanes, from sea level to 2,000 m a.s.l. It is possible
find it on slopes, fields and open forests. It feeds mainly on insects (ants and
larvae); It nests between October and November in holes in ravines, steep slopes, and cut paths.
Nests contain 5-6 eggs (Couve et al. 2016).
It is not classified in the national conservation category according to current national legislation.
At a global level it is classified as “Least Concern” (BirdLife International 2016a).

m) Coragyps atratus
The black-headed jote (Coragyps atratus) is a species of bird belonging to the order Cathartiformes.
and family Cathartidae. It is distributed from Arica to Aysén, from sea level to 2,000 m a.s.l. If
feeds on carrion and in groups. It reproduces between the months of September and October, with a posture
of 2 white eggs with red spots.
At a global level it is considered in the “Least Concern” category (BirdLife International 2016b), in Chile
It is not classified according to the criteria of the Hunting Law or RCE.
n) Geranoaetus polyosoma
The harrier (Geranoaetus polyosoma), is a species of bird belonging to the order Accipitriformes and
family Accipitridae. It is distributed from Venezuela to Chile and Argentina. In Chile from the region of
Arica and Parinacota to the Magallanes region (Tierra del Fuego) from sea level to 4,500
m.s.n.m. (Muñoz et al. 2004). Its feeding is adaptable and opportunistic, capturing mainly
rodents, also small rabbits and birds. It begins to reproduce at 4 or 5 years, its breeding period
Reproduction is very variable depending on its location, it builds nests on trees, bushes, cacti or in
crags, laying one to three eggs. Incubation lasts 28 days, with the male and female incubating
(Muñoz et al. 2004).
At the local level it is not evaluated so it does not present any conservation category according to the
current regulations, however, it is classified as a beneficial species for the activity
silvoagropecuaria (B) and as beneficial for maintaining the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
At a global level it is classified as “Least Concern” (BirdLife International 2016c).
o) Glaucidium nana
The chuncho (Glaucidium nana) is a species of native bird belonging to the order Strigiformes and family
Strigidae. In Chile it is distributed from the Atacama region to the Magallanes region (Tierra del
Fire) from sea level to 2,000 meters above sea level. (Martínez and González 2017). Wide range of
environments, including bushes, coast, forests, thorns, parks and gardens. It is a bird of prey, capable of
hunt small prey such as birds, mammals and reptiles, and even insects (Martínez and González 2017).
At the local level it is not evaluated so it does not present any conservation category according to the
current regulations, however, it is classified as a beneficial species for the activity
silvoagropecuaria (B) and as beneficial for maintaining the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
At a global level it is classified as “Least Concern” (BirdLife International 2016d).

p) Larus dominicanus
The Dominican gull (Larus dominicanus) is a bird native to Chile belonging to the order
Charadriiformes and the family Laridae. It is a bird with a wide distribution throughout Chile, ranging from
the Arica - Parinacota region to Cape Horn. It lives along the entire Chilean coast and towards the
interior of rivers. Their diet is based on fish, shellfish and crustaceans; in places where the seagulls
Dominicans cohabit with humans has become omnivorous, feeding on waste
food, in addition to this there are times that chicks and eggs neglected by their parents become
form part of the diet of this bird. Their preferred nesting site is flat areas on islands.
rocky or coastal cliffs, there are also times that they nest on the roofs of coastal buildings. By
Clutch lays 2 to 3 greenish-cream eggs with black spots.
Its international conservation status is “Least Concern” according to IUCN (BirdLife International).
2018b) and in Chile it is not classified according to the criteria of the Hunting Law or RCE.
q) Laterallus jamaicensis
The pedincito (Laterallus jamaicensis) is a species native to Chile, belonging to the order Gruiformes and

during the reproduced period
ctive. It generally lives very hidden among the vegetation, which makes its

reeds and cattails; also in flooded lands and marshes with more dispersed vegetation. From the level
from the sea up to 600 meters above sea level.
Both at a global level by the IUCN (BirdLife International 2021), and at a national level by the RCE (D.S.

r) Milvago chimango
The tiuque (Milvago chimango) is a species of native bird belonging to the order Falconiformes and family
Falconidae. In Chile it is distributed from the Atacama region to the Magallanes region (islands to the south
of the Beagle Channel) from sea level to 2,000 meters above sea level. (Torres-Murra et al. 2011). Wide range
of environments, including urban areas and coastal areas. It nests in trees and bushes, even on the ground.
It is an opportunistic and successful bird, capable of hunting small prey such as lizards and insects, raiding nests
of small birds and even eat carrion (Torres-Murra et al. 2011).
At the local level it is not evaluated so it does not present any conservation category according to the
current regulations, however, it is classified as a beneficial species for the activity
silvoagropecuaria (B) and as beneficial for maintaining the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
At a global level it is classified as “Least Concern” (BirdLife International 2018c)

s) Parabuteo unicinctus
The Peuco (Parabuteo unicinctus) is a native species of bird belonging to the order Accipitriforme and
family Accipitridae. It is widely distributed in Chile, from the Arica and Parinacota region.
to the Aysén region, being more frequent in its central-southern range. It lives from the coast to the
foothills, reaching 2,200 meters above sea level. It occupies open areas with bush and scrub vegetation.
It also uses wooded, forestry, agricultural and urban environments. It does not go up to the mountain range,
reaching the limits of the tree vegetation. Hunter, its diet is opportunistic, being able to capture
prey alone or in groups. It can feed on poultry, pigeons, rabbits and rodents,
reptiles, amphibians and even invertebrates. The reproductive period begins in August with the process
of courtship. Egg laying occurs from September to December, as a lay of one to five
eggs, the most common being two to three eggs of very soft light blue tones with slight lilac spots
or coffees. The female incubates longer than the male. It usually chooses tall, dense thorns for the
nest construction.
At a global level it is considered in the “Least Concern” category by the IUCN (BirdLife International).
2016e). At the local level it has not been evaluated, so it does not have a conservation category according to the
current national regulations, however, it is classified as a beneficial species for the
forestry and agricultural activity (B) and as beneficial for maintaining the balance of ecosystems
natural (E) in accordance with the SAG protection criteria.
t) Patagioenas araucana
The pigeon dove (Patagioenas araucana) is a species of native bird belonging to the order Columbiformes and
family Columbidae. In Chile it is distributed from the Atacama region to the Magallanes region
(Couve et al; 2016). Its environment is associated with wooded or wooded areas, also on forest edges and
cultivated areas. It is a bird closely linked to the history of Chile and pre-Columbian, this bird was greatly affected
due to an emerging disease that had it on the brink of extinction. It feeds on fruits and berries, usually
nest between December and May, the lay only consists of a white egg (online consultation at
Birds of Chile).
At the local level it is not evaluated so it does not present any conservation category according to the
current regulations, however, it is classified as a beneficial species for the activity
silvoagropecuaria (B) and as beneficial for
to the maintenance of the balance of natural ecosystems (E).
At a global level it is classified as “Least Concern” (BirdLife International 2016f).
u) Strix rufipes
The concón (Strix rufipes) is a species of bird of prey native to Chile belonging to the order Strigiformes and
family Strigidae. It is distributed from the Valparaíso region to Tierra del Fuego in the Magallanes region,
from sea level to 1,000 meters above sea level. It inhabits areas of mature, humid, and dense forests of
preference without undergrowth. It feeds on micromammals with arboreal or terrestrial habits, and its

Diet consists of lagomorphs, amphibians, reptiles and arthropods. Nests using tree cavities
mature, they use the same nests each year, the lay is 1-2 eggs of sub-elliptic shape and colored
white (Couve et al. 2016, Martínez and González 2017).
At a global level it is considered in the “Least Concern” category by the IUCN (BirdLife International).
2016g) and in Chile it is classified as “Almost threatened” by the RCE (D.S. N°16/2016 MMA).
v) Systellura longirostris
The blind hen (Systellura longirostris) is a species of the class birds belonging to the order
Caprimulgiformes and family Caprimulgidae, their distribution is continuous from the Atacama region to Aysén
(Guatecas Islands) and discontinuous for the rest of Chilean Patagonia, associated with a geographical altitude of
between sea level and 2,500 meters above sea level. Its habitat is associated with open areas, bushes and forests. It is
insectivorous and uses a modified vibrissa-like feather to capture insects resembling
a network. It nests in the month of November at ground level, depositing 2 oval eggs.
At a global level it is considered in the “Least Concern” category by the IUCN (BirdLife International).
2016h); In Chile it is not classified according to the criteria of the Hunting Law or RCE of the Ministry of
Environment or its predecessors.
w) Theristicus melanopis
The common bandurria (Theristicus melanopis), is a species of bird native to Chile belonging to the order
Pelecaniformes and family Threskiornithidae. In the country it is distributed from Antofagasta to Tierra del
Fire in the Magallanes region. It can be found in swampy areas, on the shores of lakes and lagoons and
in plowed and sown fields. It feeds opportunistically, consuming invertebrates and
small vertebrates. Nests in dispersed, not very large colonies, and can be located in trees
heights or cliffs. The lay varies between two to three elongated sub-elliptical white eggs.
yellowish (Couve et al. 2016).
It is listed globally as “Least Concern” by the IUCN (BirdLife International).
2017), it is also classified in Chile as “Least Concern” due to the RCE (D.S. N°06/2017).

y) Vanellus chilensis
The queltehue (Vanellus chilensis), is a species of native bird belonging to the order Charadriiformes and
family Charadriidae. In Chile it is distributed from Antofagasta to the Isla Grande de Chiloé in the region
of Los Lagos, from sea level to 2,200 meters above sea level. It is possible to find it in vegas, prairies,
steppes, agricultural environments and even squares and parks in cities (Couve et al. 2016). It feeds on
terrestrial or aquatic invertebrates or aquatic vertebrates, depending on the environment where they occur.
find. Its reproductive period begins early at the beginning of July, that is, in the middle of

greenish eggs with dark spots (Martínez and González 2017).
At a global level it is classified as “Least Concern” by the IUCN (BirdLife International).
2020b). At the national level, this species does not have a conservation category by the RCE according to legislation.
current national law, but it is specified as a beneficial species for forestry and agricultural activity (B)
and beneficial for maintaining the balance of natural ecosystems (E), in accordance with criteria of
the Hunting Law (2018).
z) Veniliornis lignarius
The woodpecker (Veniliornis lignarius) is a species of native bird, belonging to the order Piciforme and to the
family Picidae. In Chile it is distributed from the Coquimbo Region to the Magallanes Region. Usually
found in scattered trees, in meadows, slopes of hills, orchards, plantations and wooded areas.
Its diet is based mainly on worms, larvae and insects that it looks for between cracks in trunks or
drilling rotten trees. It begins to nest in October in the central provinces and towards summer
In the rainy areas of the south, their nests are made by making holes in trees or thick branches which
They fill with sawdust and small branches, laying 3 to 4 white, shiny, elongated eggs per clutch.
Its international conservation status is “Least Concern” according to IUCN (BirdLife International).
2016i.). In Chile it is not classified by the RCE, however, it is considered with densities
reduced population (S) according to the SAG protection criteria.

aa) Zenaida auriculata
The turtle dove (Zenaida auriculata) is a species of native bird belonging to the order Columbiformes and

(Tierra del Fuego) from sea level to 2,000 meters above sea level. It is described as the most common of the

cc) Abrothrix longipilis

frugivore and granivore mainly (Iriarte 2008).
At the local level it is evaluated as “Least Concern” according to the 8th RCE process (D.S
No. 19/2012 of MMA). At a global level it is classified as “Least Concern” (D'elia et al. 2016).

The quique (Galictis cuja) is a species native to Chile of the order Carnivora and family Mustelidae. If
distributed in Argentina, Paraguay, Uruguay, west and south of Bolivia, south and southeast of Brazil, southeast of Peru,

aggressive, but at the same time easily tamed, active throughout the day, and with solitary habits, although
It can be found in small family groups. It is an excellent digger, building galleries
long, up to four meters, whose entrance is located between bushes or quilas. Although it is common
found in native forests and forest plantations, it seems to prefer open habitats, ravines
covered with bushes, and rocky places close to water courses. It feeds mainly on
small mammal
ros and lagomorphs, and to a lesser extent other vertebrates.

gg) Histiotus magellanicus
The southern long-eared bat (Histiotus magellanicus) is a species of mammal native to the country of
order Chiroptera and family Vespertilionidae, first, described as a subspecies of H.
montanus, but identified through subsequent morphological studies as a full species. It is a
species endemic to South America, only present in Chile and Argentina. Its distribution in Chile is specified
between the Maule region and the Magallanes region. It is a medium-sized bat, characterized by
a dark brown dorsal and ventral coat; wing membranes and uropatagium almost black; ears
large and separated, without a joining band between them, features that differentiate it from H. montanus. You will
It is mainly associated with humid forests such as the Valdivian temperate forests and the
Magellanic subpolar forest, but also the sclerophyllous forest of the central zone and Patagonian steppe,
Additionally, it can use forest plantations as foraging habitat. As a refuge he usually uses
tree holes, caves and homes. It is a species with exclusively insectivorous habits. The calls
echolocation devices present FM-type pulses, with an average maximum energy frequency of 29.2
kHz.
At a global level it is in the “Least Concern, LC” category by the IUCN (Barquez & Diaz 2016) and
In Chile it is classified according to the RCE criteria as “Insufficient data” (D.S N°06/2017
MMA).

It is found in mountainous environments, humid and temperate forests, scrubland, savanna and near

kk) Lycalopex culpaeus

Family Canidae. It is distributed from Arica to Magallanes in a wide altitudinal limit and a great variety
of environments. It basically feeds on small mammals, eggs, fruits and seeds. The females
They are monoestric with a gestation period of 65 days, with a number of offspring of 3-5 puppies. They receive care
biparental (Iriarte 2008). It has a twilight behavior and generally hunts at night.
In accordance with national legislation, it presents a conservation category as “Concern
minor” according to the fifth process of the RCE (D.S. Nº33/2011). At a global level, there is also
categorized as “Least Concern” according to the IUCN (Lucherini 2016.).

nn) Pudu puda
The pudú (Pudu puda) is a native species of the order Artiodactyla and the family Cervidae, the largest deer.
small from Chile. Its distribution in the country is specified between the regions of Maule and Aysén, it is described
as a solitary species or forming groups of up to 3 individuals. It is found in mountainous areas

oo) Puma concolor
The puma (Puma concolor) is a native species of the order Carnivora and the family Felidae, the largest feline.
great of Chile. Its distribution in the country is specified throughout the continental territory, between the regions
from Arica and Parinacota and Magallanes, is described as a territorial and solitary species that occupies large

of small, medium and large size and carrion, native and introduced species. The females present
two mating seasons: from January to February and from August to September; although there are no breeding seasons
specific. They have 1 to 4 young per litter.
At a global level it is considered in the “Least Concern” category by the IUCN (Nielsen et al. 2015) and in
Chile is classified as “Near Threatened” by the RCE (D.S. N°42/2011).
3.1.5.5 Susceptibility to the effects of Climate Change
According to the Climate Risk Atlas (ARCLIM-MMA), there is a high susceptibility to change
climate at the ecosystem level (loss of flora and fauna due to changes in temperature and precipitation), in
the commune of Penco, where the area of influence of the Project is located.
Regarding the adverse effects on the distribution of biodiversity of fauna species, product
of the future change in the conditions of average annual precipitation and average annual temperature,
a “high” value is estimated for the risk index of loss of faunal biodiversity due to changes in
Precipitation and temperature for the commune of Penco.
This value was obtained considering the threat index due to the effect of decreased rainfall
and the increase in future average temperature (2035-2065), with respect to climatic conditions
historical (1980-2010), with results of “moderate” and “very low” for the associated impact chains
a loss of biodiversity due to changes in precipitation and loss of biodiversity due to changes in
temperature respectively. In the case of the intervention level variable, understood as the exposure
or the degree of loss of natural vegetation surface that the territory has experienced in the last 30
years which is related to the increase in the sensitivity of species to climate changes, the index
is “very high” for both impact chains. Finally, the vulnerability index or margin of
security (climatic tolerance) and adaptive capacity (precipitation and temperature) appears “low”
for precipitation change and “very low” for temperature changes.

In the case of susceptibility to climate change at the species level, in ARCLIM it was possible to obtain
distribution models for five species of interest to the Project, for a historical period (1980-2010)
and future (2035-2065), the species evaluated correspond to Eupsophus roseus, (Vulnerable), Liolaemus
schroederi (Vulnerable), Dromiciops gliroides (Near Threatened), Pudu puda (Near Threatened) and Puma
concolor (Near threatened). The species selection criterion does not only obey categories of
classification, but also to the sensitivity of the species in relation to natural characteristics such as
degree of mobility, ecosystem role and potential interaction with the project among other factors.
In this regard, in the case of Eupsophus roseus the ARCLIM model shows that the probability of
future presence decreases slightly from the Biobío Region towards the north, mostly in
the coastal zone and the Andes Mountains, considering a climate scenario with intense emissions
of greenhouse gases. On the contrary, it can be seen in Figure 3-74 that the projection
future presence increases considerably both in the Araucanía Region and in the Region of
Los Ríos, which could be interpreted as an improvement in the basal conditions that would favor the
establishment of the species. In particular, in the area of influence of the Project, the probability of
presence of the species presents a decrease of 25.5% in a future period.

3.1.6 Synthesis and Conclusions
The area of influence of the Project for terrestrial vertebrate fauna is made up of four environments,
corresponding to forest, scrub, modified and plantation. Such environments are in accordance with
the vegetation formations recorded by the flora and vegetation component, because they were
identified and/or established based on those.
The collection of information in the identified environments was carried out through five campaigns
of terrain, covering the four seasonalities; autumn, winter, spring and summer; the first among
on June 13 and 17, 2022 (autumn); the second between August 15 and 19, 2022 (winter); the
third between October 3 and 7, 2022 (spring); the fourth between January 9 and 13,
2023 (summer) and the fifth between October 2 and 6, 2023 (spring). Each work day had
a duration of approximately 10 effective hours and was attended by three teams of two
3-107
==================================================================================
PAGE 123
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
specialists in wildlife studies in each field campaign, totaling around 300
hours of work per field campaign. In addition to the baseline, visits were made
of land for surveying target species. In this way, specific information was obtained from
the species Dromiciops gliroides (trapping between March 20 and 25) and medium-sized mammals (analysis with
camera traps between January to July 2023).
Regarding the sampling stations, 31 points were implemented for each field campaign. In
each of these transects, specific stations for birds and directed search for the
detection of amphibians, reptiles, birds and mammals (PMF Type 1: points where the techniques were applied
standard for different taxa). The methods of camera traps, Sherman traps, playback and
ultrasound detector were implemented in seven of the PMF (PMF Type 2: points where
apart from standard techniques, complementary techniques).
Two sites of interest for the terrestrial fauna component were recorded close to the area of influence of the
project, corresponding to the Nonguén National Park located approximately 8 km south of the
project and the Hualpén Peninsula Nature Sanctuary, located approximately 13 km in the direction
west of the Project. However, due to the distance and the nature of the works and actions involved,
None of these areas are likely to be affected by the Project.
From the collection of information on the ground, the presence of 73 species of fauna was recorded
native terrestrial (48 records in autumn 2022, 48 in winter 2022, 49 in spring 2022, 57 in summer
2023 and 47 in spring 2023), corresponding to two amphibians, six reptiles, 49 birds and 16 mammals.
In relation to the categories of conservation, endemism, degree of mobility, migratory status and
BSE criteria, of the total number of registered species (n= 73), the following can be indicated:
• Conservation category: 29 (39.1% of the total) are in the conservation category according to
current national legislation: two amphibians, six reptiles, eight birds and 13 mammals. three of them
They are in the threatened category (one amphibian, one reptile and two mammals).
• Endemism: six species of terrestrial vertebrates with exclusive distribution in our country (one
amphibian, a reptile, three birds and a mammal), which correspond to 8.2% of the total species
registered.
• Mobility: 13 are of low mobility (two amphibians, six reptiles and five small mammals) and 60 of
high mobility (49 birds and 11 mammals).
• Migratory status: 54 species, 74% of the total; are residents, while 14 species migrate
within the national territory, two migrate outside the national territory and three species (chiropterans)
are migratory, but there are no scientific studies that indicate whether they do so outside or inside the
country.
• BSE criteria: 45 species classified as beneficial for forestry and agricultural activity (B)
(one reptile, 36 birds and eight mammals), 15 species cataloged with population densities
reduced (S) (one amphibian, three reptiles, eight birds and three mammals) and 35 species classified as

beneficial for maintaining the balance of natural ecosystems (E) (two amphibians, six

Regarding the records by campaign and environments, the following is indicated:
• In the autumn 2022 campaign, 17 species were recorded in the forest environment, corresponding
15 species of birds and two of mammals. In the scrub environment, 20 species were recorded, two
species of reptiles and 18 species of birds. In the modified environment, 23 species were observed, an amphibian,
a reptile and 21 birds. Finally, the richest environment corresponds to plantations, with 37
species, distributed in four reptiles, 27 birds and six mammals.
• In the 2022 winter campaign, 22 species were recorded in the forest environment,
corresponding to a reptile, 19 species of birds and
two mammals. In the scrub environment
They recorded 26 species, two species of reptiles, 23 of birds and one mammal. In the environment
modified, 29 species were observed, two reptiles, 25 birds and two mammals. Lastly, the
The richest environment corresponds to the plantation, with 42 species, distributed in four
reptiles, 30 birds and eight mammals.
• In the spring 2022 campaign, 15 species were recorded in the forest environment,
corresponding to 12 species of birds and three of mammals. In the scrub environment
They recorded 24 species, one species of reptile, 22 birds and one mammal. In the environment
modified, 26 species were observed, three reptiles and 23 birds. Finally, the environment of greater
richness corresponds to plantation, with 44 species, distributed in four reptiles, 35 birds and five
mammals.
• In the 2023 summer campaign, 26 species were recorded in the forest environment,
corresponding to three reptiles, 20 species of birds and three of mammals. In the bush environment
24 species were recorded, four species of reptiles and 20 of birds. In the modified environment
They observed 22 species, four reptiles and 18 birds. Finally, the richest environment
corresponds to a plantation, with 50 species, distributed in one amphibian, five reptiles, 36 birds and
eight mammals.
• And in the spring 2023 campaign, 22 species were recorded in the forest environment,
corresponding to five reptiles, 15 species of birds and two of mammals. In the environment
scrubland, 27 species were recorded, three reptiles and 24 birds. In the modified environment
They observed 28 species, five reptiles and 23 birds. Finally, the richest environment
corresponds to plantation, with 41 species, distributed in five reptiles, 32 birds and four
mammals.
With respect to biodiversity indices, it is possible to mention that the environments with greater diversity,
According to the Shannon and Simpson indices, they are planted in the spring 2023 campaign (H'=2.09) and
scrub in the spring 2022 campaign (D=0.89).
With respect to the rarefaction curve, it appears that 85% of the
total estimated (theoretical) richness, with an observed richness of 73 of a total of 86 species, according to the

Chao 2 estimator, which considers sampling sufficiency, considering this a sampling
representative of the area of influence.
Of the total number of species recorded during the five sampling campaigns, 40 have been considered as
species of interest and/or sensitive. Of these, 11 correspond to species of interest (two amphibians, two
reptiles, two birds and five mammals) and 37 are sensitive species (two amphibians, seven reptiles, 18 birds and 10
mammals).
Finally, in relation to the risk map for the loss of fauna biodiversity associated with the
susceptibility to climate change at the species level, the risk was high for both the variable Loss
of fauna biodiversity due to changes in precipitation and for the Loss of fauna biodiversity
due to temperature changes in the commune of Penco. Distribution models were obtained for five
species of interest to the Project in ARCLIM, for a historical period (1980-2010) and future (2035-2065).
Regarding the species evaluated, the preliminary model of historical presence versus future presence granted
as a result, a decrease in the probability of presence of 25% for the species Eupsophus
roseus, 10% for Dromiciops gliroides, 8.5% for Pudu puda and 4.8% for Puma concolor. On the other hand, the
Liolaemus schroederi species presents a positive safety margin, with a 4% increase in the
probability of occurrence of the species in the Biobío Region.

3.2 Invertebrate Fauna
3.2.1 Introduction
Below is the background information on the Terrestrial Invertebrate Fauna Baseline.
(Arthropoda), within the framework of the environmental evaluation of the “Mining Development Project for the Extraction of
Clays for the Production of Rare Earth Concentrate” (hereinafter the Project). According to the Law on Bases
General Environment Regulations (Law No. 19,300/1994), the environment is defined as “the global system
Consisting of natural and artificial elements of a physical, chemical or biological nature, and their
interactions, in permanent modification by human or natural action and that governs and conditions the
existence and development of life in its multiple manifestations.” Invertebrates as part of the
ecosystem and environment have gained significance due to the different functionalities (diversity
functional) established in the different studies developed to date. In addition to this, it must
consider the potential presence of invertebrate fauna species with conservation problems,
considered in part by the Species Classification Regulation (RCE, hereinafter).
By virtue of the works and actions of the Project, a characterization study of the
invertebrates present in the area of influence, where they determine the species present, characterize them
according to the state of conservation in which they are found and associate their state, as a community,
to the vegetation conditions provided by the different habitats present for this
component.
This document describes
ben and present the findings, distribution of invertebrates and indices that
help predict the state of the invertebrate component, providing information through
indices: QBS-ar and diversity evaluators (Shannon et al.). Additionally, the abundance
and richness to be able to analyze the diversity of invertebrates of the sites of interest in the area of
influence of the Project, considering what is indicated in the “Guide to the Description of Soil Components,
flora and fauna of terrestrial ecosystems in the SEIA” (SEA 2015) and the “Guide to evaluation criteria in the
SEIA: Technical criteria for terrestrial fauna field campaigns and data validation” (SEA, 2022).
3.2.2 Objectives
3.2.2.1 General Objective
The general objective is to quantitatively and qualitatively characterize the current state of the fauna
terrestrial invertebrate present in the area of influence of the Project.
3.2.2.2 Specific Objectives
• Characterize and describe biogeographic aspects that have an impact on the diversity of the
entomofauna in which the Project is inserted.

• Identify the species of invertebrate fauna present in the area of influence of the Project in
regarding its richness and state of conservation.
• Represent ecological indices and diversity indices for the invertebrate community
observed.
• Identify the presence of invertebrate fauna in the conservation category in accordance with the
Species Classification Regulation (RCE).
3.2.3 Area of Influence
The Project area is located mainly in the Commune of Penco, and part of the surface of the areas
Maite and Luna extraction site is located in the commune of Concepción, both in the Province of
Concepción, Biobío Region. For the definition and description of the area of influence of the fauna component
terrestrial invertebrate, the guidelines of the “Guide for the description of the area of influence in
the SEIA” (SEA, 2017), which consider the following aspects: preliminary identification of possible
effects, spatial extension of the parts, works and/or activities of the Project, the geographical space of the
elements receiving impacts, relevant attributes and current situation. Once these are defined
aspects, the area of influence was established where the effects on the component can manifest
invertebrate fauna product of the parts and works of the Project, such as the development of its activities.
For the definition and description of the area of influence of the terrestrial invertebrate fauna component,
followed the guidelines of the “Guide for the description of the soil, flora and fauna components of
terrestrial ecosystems in the SEIA” (2015) and the “Guide for the description of the area of influence in the SEIA”
(2017), which consider the following aspects: preliminary identification of possible effects,
spatial extension of the parts, works and/or activities of the project, the geographical space of the elements
impact receptors, relevant attributes and current situation. Once these aspects are defined,
established the area of influence where the effects on the fauna component can manifest
product of the Project works and the development of its activities.
The area where the Project will be located corresponds to a sector located east of Route 150, and has a
intervention area of 169.19 hectares. A buffer is included in this area considering a width
approximately 200m from the limit of the Project areas towards the outside, forming a polygon with
a total area of 673.62 hectares (Figure 3-79). The buffer area is greater than the Project area, since
considers an additional surface area that could potentially be affected by the development of the
Project activities, considering the inherent displacement capacity that the project has.
fauna component. Annex 3B-42 presents the AI ​​of the component in digital format.

3.2.4 Methodology
3.2.4.1 Study area
i. Geomorphological characteristics in the area of influence
The study area, considering that it is immersed in the commune of Penco and to a lesser extent in the
commune of Concepción, Bío-bío Region, is located in a geomorphological unit known as
fluvial/alluvial sedimentation plain, which is characterized by its flat appearance, slightly
undulated and irrigated by the action of the waters of the present hydrographic basins. This condition
favors forestry exploitation, which is present throughout the study area. The ripples
Orographic features allow the generation of ravines in the relief where the accumulation of native flora
allows to improve the attraction of native arthropods.
ii. Vegetative characteristics of the area of influence
According to the national forest inventory (INFOR 2023), the Project area has an extensive
area of forest plantations, in total, 4,377 hectares of forest exploitation, dominating the landscape of
the sampling points. Species such as Eucalyptus globulus (2,765 ha) and Pine are mainly observed.
radiata (1,241 ha), which protect innumerable refuges for the species prospected in the
surface of the trunks, in the galleries that can occur in old trunks, in the leaf litter that
generates on the ground. The natural-native vegetation, whether forest associations or shrublands, are found
to a lesser extent distributed in the area of influence, as can be seen in Figure 3-80.

3.2.4.2 Field activities
i. Sampling design
Based on the surface area of project work sites, a remote sensing process is generated.
satellite through the Landviewer platform, where chromatic bands can be analyzed. Of this
Likewise, the infrared band was analyzed to determine the existing vegetation, in conjunction with the
bands that generate the vegetation cover index NDVI, (Normalized Difference Vegetation Index),
translated into Spanish as the healthy vegetation index
and. This index is a standardized measure that
is used to assess the health and density of vegetation on the Earth's surface using
information captured by remote sensors that detect electromagnetic radiation in different
wavelengths, such as visible and near-infrared light. For this, the images of the
Sentinel-2 and Land 8 satellites, which guarantee high-quality images in spatial resolution for
3-125
==================================================================================
PAGE 141
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
detect fine variations in vegetation cover; better capture multispectral data, which
which means that they allow better precision and sensitivity to reflect the amount of vegetation and its
health status; They have a scheduled Earth check frequency, which means they can
taking images of a specific area every few days or weeks allowing for long-term monitoring
of the vegetation and to be able to evaluate temporal changes. And finally, both Sentinel-2 from the Space Agency
European Union (ESA) and NASA/USGS Landsat 8 offer freely accessible data.
A timelapse process or also called time lapse process was developed, which is a
videography technique that consists of capturing a sequence of images in time intervals
and play them at a faster speed than they were captured. Thanks to this technique
managed to compare changes in vegetation cover in the NDVI index over two years during the season
of spring. The resulting variations allow us to define areas where the vegetation is more stable and,
therefore, with a greater probability of finding and observing entomofauna.
This information allows the selection of the different sampling units carried out in the field (point
entomological sampling, PME from now on) determining different assemblages of each of these
variations that each of the environments in which the Project is established represents, considering
In addition, some physiographic formations of interest, such as ravines or significant reliefs that
can host specific entomofauna of these microenvironments.
a) Summer campaign

ii. Study design
In accordance with the objectives of this study, the characterization of invertebrates was carried out under two
methods, an active collection and a passive collection, distributed in each of the prospected sites
called entomofauna sampling points (EPS from now on). This sampling was established by
means a systematic stratified methodology, establishing as sampling units the different
PME, which includes a transect 200 meters long by 4 meters wide and installations
traps. In each PME the surface was walked on foot with the help of an odometer and
an active collection under different rocks and pieces of logs available in the area that make up each one
of the transects carried out.
In the case of the bushes and trees that were prospected, an entomological umbrella was used, whose
function mainly consists of tapping on branches and woody structures available from each
transect, generating the fall and subsequent collection with an entomological aspirator of the different species
that can be observed in the white cloth blanket that makes up the entomological umbrella used, whose
surface was one square meter. In the particular case of cultivated trees, the inspection was carried out
under bark, where present individuals were collected, and in each of the PME a tour was carried out
collecting flying insects through the entomological network. For passive collection,
Barber and pitfall traps, where in each PME, it was installed following the recommendations to avoid
typical errors that can affect the data obtained with the auto-correlation between the samples according to
Lafranco et al (2010). For this purpose, pitfall traps (pitfall and barber traps) were placed every 40 meters.
along the transect. The pitfall traps were made up of a 360 degree plastic cup.
ml buried at the surface and the Barber traps were similarly arranged as the pitfall traps,
where a third of its capacity was filled with aromatic liquid bait based on apple cider vinegar and grapes.
red those that were installed randomly in each PME, the permanence time of these traps
It was at least 24 hours average.
For the study area, which involves closed polygonals, in order to provide an adequate
spatial representativeness both in land occupation, physiography of the relief, and formations
vegetation defined in the flora and vegetation baseline, the summer campaign was established
prospecting of 6 PME and for the winter campaign the representation of 7 PME was carried out, which
Involves both summer and winter transects with a total of 36 traps
pitfall and 36 chromatic traps.

To represent the nocturnal entomofauna of the project, two methodologies were carried out: (1) Walk
aimed at searching for scorpions with UV flashlights, which were carried out around the station
night collection. (2) Night collection station; where a lighting system was installed with
different types of light (different radiation and different electromagnetic waves) which will be
projected onto a white sheet which was attached vertically by means of an aluminum frame and
fixed to the ground. All this from 9:00 p.m. to 1:00 a.m. approx. The insects collected under that
system were collected in small plastic pots with lids, where they were counted, classified
and the easily identifiable species were released, as well as the species that had already been collected. For
preserve the collected Lepidoptera, they were kept in entomological envelopes to avoid damage to their wings
and scales, ensuring the preservation of diagnostic structures that allow the identification of
species.

3.2.4.3 Collection, conservation and identification of species
The collection and conservation of entomofauna specimens is subject to the different taxa to which
which ones belong. In general, two collection lines were followed, one for active collection and the other
by passive collection. In active collection, individuals were stored in plastic containers with lids.
and were labeled with information on the collection points. The easily identified species were
registered and released individuals. In the case of taxa that are difficult to identify with the naked eye, the
Individuals were transferred to the laboratory for subsequent identification under stereoscopic magnifying glass.
(Figure 3-86) and were deposited in JVR's personal reference collection. In the case of the collection
passive, individuals were removed from the traps, labeled and stored in containers for disposal.
transfer and subsequent identification in the laboratory. More details on the conservation and identification of
The main classes and/or orders are described below:
• Entognatha Class (=Hexapoda): All specimens were preserved in 90° alcohol, properly
packaged and labeled. The determination of the specimens was carried out with the use
of the key of orders and families of Sendra (2015), Bernava (2008) and key of collembolos of
Jordana, R. and Arbea, J. (1989). For these purposes, the identification was carried out both with a magnifying glass
stereoscopic where it was possible to separate specimens of this order and with the use of a microscope
determined the family or order
• Class Arachnida, order Araneae: All specimens were preserved in 90° alcohol,
preserved in different bottles and labeled according to the collection point. For the
identification, at the family level we mainly followed Grismado et al. (2014) and for
To determine the genera and species, the world catalog of Arachnida “World Spider” was accessed.
Catalog”, where you have free access to any scientific work or paper related to
the taxonomy of the order Araneae. In this way an observation is made under a magnifying glass.
stereoscopic inspecting structures of a diagnostic nature, both for individuals
females, juveniles and males; observing arrangement of genitalia, bristles, setae or
hairs on
different structures, eye configuration among other specific characters for each family,
genus and/or species.
• Class Arachnida, order Solifugae: All specimens were preserved in 90° alcohol. For the
determination at the family level was followed by Iuri (2023) and the systematic review in Botero's thesis
2018 (data not yet published)
• Class Insecta, order Lepidoptera: Both diurnal and nocturnal butterflies were collected
with the use of the entomological net with a 90 cm long stick and a 50 cm ring. He became immobilized
to the specimens by squeezing the thorax with the index fingers and thumb held firm by some
seconds, in order to prevent the wings from bending backwards and losing scales, which
3-132
==================================================================================
PAGE 148
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
It would generate damage to its structures that makes correct identification difficult. Once immobilized
90° alcohol is injected into the abdomen using a syringe and stored in paper envelopes.
diamond. For identification, it is necessary to have completely
stretched, therefore, they soften in laboratory work. For this a camera is prepared
of moisture based on acetic acid and sand. The specimens are left inside the chamber
humidity for at least 4 hours at 20°C. Then the envelopes with the already soft specimens are removed.
and proceed to stretch the wings, using entomological pin number 2 and a wing extender.
12° butterflies. The wings are fixed with the rear edge of them at 90° with respect to the line of the
body. In this way, both hind wings and wings can be seen with a stereoscopic magnifying glass.
previous, being able to follow specific keys of families and genres as appropriate. The
Works reviewed for this order were: Angulo & Weigent (1975) and Peña (2006).
• Class Insecta, order Hemiptera: The ground bugs were transported in sealed jars
airtight tight with cotton to prevent loss of the oral apparatus and other structures that
They allow, later in the laboratory, to determine families. To review cicadas, we followed
the key to the genus Tettigades by Torres (1968) and the work of Lukasik et al. (2017). For others
groups within this order were followed: Camponodico (2021) and Fuentes-Contreras et al. (1997).
• Class Insecta, order Coleoptera: The beetles collected at the site of interest were separated
through a magnifying glass of the terrain, determining the different families involved and processed in
laboratory to determine the different species where available literature and keys were used
such as Kaszab (1978), Vidal (2007) and Solervicens (2014).
• Class Insecta, order Thysanoptera: The specimens of this group were collected and stored in
bottles with 70° alcohol and some in airtight bottles with cotton to avoid loss of
certain scales and other diagnostic structures for the identification of families and genera.
• Class Insecta, order Diptera: The collected specimens were preserved in 90° alcohol and
identified under stereoscopic magnifying glass following Mello-Patiu (2015), Barahona-Segovia et al.
(2021), Brncic (1957), Coscarón & Gonzales (1991), Gonzáles et al. (2017), O'hara et al. (2021) and
Raphael (2001).
• Class Insecta, order Hymenoptera: The specimens collected and fallen mainly in the
chromatic trays, were separated and cleaned with detergent. Later they were
dried at 40°C for 1 hour and “combed” with a fine brush size 2/0. So the hairiness does not
would make their correct identification difficult. Later, they were deposited in collection (CJVR private col.)
with treatment of entomological pins No. 2 in a specimen stretching process. The
Keys used for this order were concentrated in the review of Chiappa (1990), Sielfeld (1973),
Toro and Rodríguez (1998), Sielfeld (1980), Vivallo et all (2003). Specific reviews were carried out

i. Photographs of collected species
In situ photographs (of live samples) were taken with a Nikon d7500 camera attached to a
Laowa 100mm 2x macro lens and adding a godox tt350 flash with a DIY diffuser for lighting and
The development was carried out in the Adobe Lightroom cc Classic (2022) program to improve visual aspects,
while for ex situ photographs (dead samples) the same camera was used but adding
a remote controller and a Laowa 25mm ultra macro 2.5-5x lens attached to a manual micrometer rail
NISI NM-180 on a vertical platform, to use the “Focus Stacking” technique, the
Processing of these images was carried out in the Helicon focus program to stack and then Photoshop (2022)
to improve visual aspects (Figure 3-87). To photograph arthropods ex situ, they must be fixed with
entomological pin and in some small individuals (less than 5mm) these must be fixed in
entomological nails with the help of glue based on gum arabic, which must be given a
drying time of at least 12 hours before photographing to avoid damage and mutilation of the
structures that make up the insect.

i. Ecological parameters
The ecological parameters allow us to read the current, future and past scenario of the site.
study. With this, strategies can be generated that lead to its conservation, determine the threats
and enhance the services that the analyzed invertebrates provide to the ecosystem under study. For this
There are different parameters that will be analyzed distinctly from the ecological indices:
Balance Number of dentritivores/Number of non-dentritivores: Posed by Cabrera-Dávila in 2014.
Where he comments that the indicators of highest soil quality are those that have values above 1
as a result of the ratio between the number of detritivores compared to the number of non-detritivores
3-135
==================================================================================
PAGE 151
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Similarity: To measure or express the degree or percentage of similarity that the samples obtained in
the prospected plots, and which may eventually represent two independent communities
or to two communities with greater distributional breadth, all based on the species that are
observed in each plot according to each prospected point, the similarity index is used with
two coefficients; Jaccard widely used to process quantitative data (Presence/Absence). and
the Bryan-Curtis coefficient to process qualitative data, that is, species (abundance). The
information contained in the similarity matrices obtained by the already mentioned indices is
represented by clusters, for a better interpretation of the results, it was used as
“Inweighted pair-group average” or “UPGMA” algorithm. The calculation and the dendrogram carried out were done with
the PAST 4 program. With this similarity analysis you can see the degree of seasonal structuring of the
arthropod assembly in
the study sites.
Wealth: From the results obtained in the PME, the specific wealth was calculated, which represents
the number of the different species recorded. This calculation was carried out in a general way for the entire area,
by class, by family and by gender.
Abundance: Relative abundance was estimated as the expression of the abundance of a species with
relation to another in magnitude (SEA, 2015). In this case it is expressed as the number of individuals in a
species, based on the total number of individuals registered for all species in a given environment,
This abundance was represented by the main groups of Arthropods among those that make up the
different classes and orders present throughout the study area. While both absolute abundance and
expression of the abundance of a species, refers to the number of individuals counted during a
sampling, but does not represent the total number of individuals belonging to a population of a given area (SEA,
2015). In this parameter, the dominance of the species and the diversity are measured using the index of
Simpson, for which the EstimateS 9.1.0® software was used (Colwell, 2014).
The calculation of relative abundance is indicated below in the following formula:
Number of individuals of the species
Relative abundance= ∗100
Number of individuals of all species
Density: The estimated density was evaluated from the absolute abundance records in each PME.
The estimated densities were obtained, which correspond to the estimate of the number of individuals
of the same species present in a defined area unit. For the purposes of this study, it was calculated
individuals/hectares (Ind/ha). The estimated density was evaluated with the formula:
ind Average number of individuals per environment

i. Species accumulation curve
One of the properties of species richness is that, for a given area, its estimated value
varies asymptotically as the sample size increases, this relationship being illustrated
by species accumulation curves, it also describes the cumulative number of species
collected based on sampling effort. Among the characteristics of the accumulation curve
is the fact that when the sampling effort tends to an arbitrarily large number, the
slope of the species accumulation curve tends to 0. When this occurs, theoretically, you want
say that the total number of species for the studied area has been reached. Therefore, the curves
accumulation methods are frequently used, both to estimate total wealth through accumulation
of an area under study, to evaluate the sufficiency of a given sampling effort.
To generate the species accumulation curves, all data that confirmed
the presence of species, considering each sampling unit (PME) as a sample. For the
preparation and modeling of the rarefaction curves, the EstimateS 9.1.0® software was used (Colwell,
2014).
ii. Conservation status of species
The conservation status of the invertebrates was determined based on information from the processes
species classification officials (up to the 19th process), these Chilean species classification instruments
species are published on the official website of the Ministry of the Environment and provide information
regarding the biology and distribution to date of species that present conservation problems.
In such a way that the species present in the study area can be projected, considering the
determining factors in its presence or absence such as distribution, preferred habitats and aspects
biological characteristics of the species itself, thus establishing a list of potential species in the area of
study.
It should be considered that the species present in a territory can be classified according to their origin
biogeographic in:
Native species: which “are those species native to the place where they live, which in the case of Chile
rises to little
or more than 30,600 species (approximately 1.5% of the species described for the
world)” (MMA, 2019). “The largest group is insects, with almost 33% of the known native species
For Chile, plants are next in importance, 23% of those described for Chile, and fungi with 11% of those described for Chile.
species cited for Chile” (MMA, 2019).
Endemic species: those that live exclusively within a certain territory, whether a
continent, a country, a political administrative region, a biogeographical region, an island or an area
particular.
3-137
==================================================================================
PAGE 153
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Exotic species: these are foreign species that have been introduced outside their distribution.
natural; species whose natural origin has taken place in another part of the world, and which for reasons
mainly anthropic have been transported to another site (voluntarily or involuntarily).
Depending on their conservation status, species can be classified as extinct, extinct in the wild,
Critically Endangered, Endangered, Vulnerable, Near Threatened, Least Concern, and Data Deficient:
Extinct: (EX): A Taxon is extinct when there is no reasonable doubt that the last individual
existing has died. A taxon is presumed extinct when exhaustive surveys of its
habitats, known and/or expected at appropriate times (daily, seasonal or annual), and throughout
Throughout its historical range, it has not been able to detect a single individual.
Extinct in the Wild (EW): a taxon is Extinct in the Wild when it only survives in
cultivation, in captivity or as naturalized populations completely outside their natural distribution.
Critically Endangered (CR): a taxon is Critically Endangered when the best available evidence indicates that
meets any of the criteria “A” through “E” for Critically Endangered and is therefore considered
which is facing an extremely high risk of extinction in the wild.
Endangered (EN): a taxon is Endangered when the best available evidence indicates that it meets
any of the criteria “A” to “E” for Endangered and is therefore considered to be facing
at a very high risk of extinction in the wild.
Vulnerable (VU): a taxon is Vulnerable when the best available evidence indicates that it meets
any of criteria “A” to “E” for Vulnerable and is therefore considered to be facing
at high risk of extinction in the wild.
Near Threatened (NT): a taxon is Near Threatened when it has been evaluated according to the criteria and is not
currently meets the criteria for Critically Endangered, Endangered or Vulnerable; but it is close to
satisfy the criteria, or possibly will satisfy them, in the near future.
Least Concern (LC): a taxon is considered Least Concern when, having been evaluated
does not meet any of the criteria that define the categories of Critically Endangered, Endangered, Vulnerable
or Almost Threatened. Abundant and widely distributed taxa are included in this category.
Data Deficient (DD): A taxon is included in the Data Deficient category when there is no
adequate information to make an assessment, direct or indirect, of their risk of extinction based on
in the distribution and/or condition of the population. A taxon in this category may be well studied, and
3-138
==================================================================================
PAGE 154
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
its biology be well known, but lack appropriate data on its abundance and/or distribution.
Insufficient Data is therefore not a threat category.

ii. Invertebrate analysis
A total of 141 species were observed and recorded. Of all of them, the presence of the class stands out
insecta, class Entognatha and class Arachnida. For the Insecta Class, 1686 individuals were observed, 35

of each of the classes observed in Figure 3-89. In turn, Figure 3-90 indicates the different
abundance percentages of the ecological attributes of the total species found during the
2023 summer campaign.

Regarding dominance as an ecological parameter, the behavior of this variable can be seen in the
Figure 3-94, where low dominance values stand out in all PME, reflecting a diversity that
uniformly uses the available resources, since there is no taxonomic element that generates
pressure or dominance that is reflected by the dominance index. This dominance index value
It also represents the probability that if 2 individuals are found in these environments (PME)
there being a 25% probability that these species are the same species in PME8 and a 24%
of probabilities in PME1. This probability is conditioned by diversity, therefore, when
When high diversity occurs, the probabilities are low.

PME2: Environment with high shelters, leaf litter soil and Eucalyptus plantations surrounded by some

an area of native flora which always responds with good acceptance for the entomofauna of the sector,
transforming this PME as the one with the best diversity.

iv. Species accumulation curve
A species accumulation analysis was carried out to verify the efficiency of the sampling effort and
thus verify the representativeness of the samples collected to the diversity of the study site in
summer campaign. For this, non-parametric estimators were used without adjustment for a model
determined. The indexes used for this representation were established to be able to process data
of presence and absence with the Jacknife1 indices and in order to relate the abundance of this study,
used the CHAO-1 and ACE indices. All these tests were performed with 1000 randomizations. These
Estimators are used for low species abundance in the samples. (Pizarro-Araya et al. (2018).
Table 3-22 is prepared, showing the results of all the non-parametric indices used.
currently, where they are related to the study sites (PME), they are averaged and the percentage is measured
of sampling efficiency, it should be considered that the Chao 2 index takes into account the species
observed in exactly one and two sampling units; Jack 1 is a function of the number of
species present in only one sampling unit.

iii. Analysis of invertebrates present
Among invertebrates, the greatest participation in terms of presence in the project areas is
Find the arthropods. Therefore, the analyzes developed here consider arthropods as the
studied invertebrates. Managing to observe and record 160 species during the campaign. Of all of them
The presence of the insecta class, Entognatha class and Arachnida class stands out. For insects, they were observed
946 individuals, Entognatha 153 individuals and Arachnida 169 individuals. Figure 3-106 shows the
participation of each of the observed classes.
The abundance of insects is concentrated with the presence of the order Coleoptera with 456 individuals, followed
by the taxonomic order Diptera with the record of 336 individuals. Regarding class orders
insecta observations concentrate individuals in 19 total orders, considering all classes
present. The number of individuals in the different orders can be seen in Figure 3-107, where
Coleoptera and Diptera stand out as dominant groups in the studied assemblage.

v. Species accumulation curve
A species accumulation analysis was carried out to verify the efficiency of the sampling effort and
thus verify the representativeness of the samples collected to the diversity of the study site in
summer campaign. For this, non-parametric estimators were used without adjustment for a model
determined. The indexes used for this representation were established to be able to process data
of presence and absence with the Jacknife1 indices and in order to relate the abundance of this study,
used the CHAO-1 and ACE indices. All these tests were performed with 1000 randomizations. These
Estimators are used for low species abundance in the samples. (Pizarro-Araya et al. 2018).
Table 3-29 is prepared, showing the results of all the non-parametric indices used.
currently, where they are related to the study sites (PME), they are averaged and the percentage is measured
of sampling efficiency, it should be considered that the Chao 2 index takes into account the species
observed in exactly one and two sampling units; Jack 1 is a function of the number of
species present in only one sampling unit, the results for winter can be observed in
Table 3-29.

vii. Functional distribution during winter
In an ecosystem, insects play a critical role by providing a number of functions
key ecological conditions that affect the structure and functioning of said ecosystem. These functions
Ecological species can be divided into different attributes, such as pollinators, detritivores, predators and
xylophages, among others. Each of which plays a specific role in the dynamics of the
ecosystem. Below, in Table 3-32, some of these attributes present during the

3.2.5.3 Background of species with conservation status
i. Ceroglossus chilensis
Table 3-34 is presented with the taxonomic information for the species.
Table 3-34 Taxonomy of Ceroglossus chilensis
Kingdom Animalia Order Coleoptera
Division Arthropoda Family Carabidae
Class Insecta Genus Ceroglossus
GAC Source
Ceroglossus Chilensis or Peroglossus corresponds to a native species present in the Maule Regions,
Biobío, La Araucanía, Los Ríos, Los Lagos and Aysén. It is associated with native and exotic forest formations in
coastal, pre-Andean and coastal mountain range sectors. There is no information about its size.
population, but it has been noted that it is a dominant species, with its relative abundance being greater in
plantations of 6-7 and 10 years than in older plantations.
It is included in the Chilean Regulations, protected by Hunting Law No. 4,601; the criteria defined by the
SAG to protect this species were based on Article 3 of said Law, being a 'catalogued species
with reduced population densities (S)' and 'beneficial for maintaining the balance of
natural ecosystems (E)’. According to the Committee, it is concluded that its Conservation Category is
Least concern (LC).
It is distinguished by its elongated and dorsoventrally flattened shape, with strongly
sclerosed and great variety of colors; Its head, thorax and elytra are greenish-black or bluish,
Ventrally they are black, as are their legs, maxillary and labial palps. Its head and pronotum are
of a bluish color and its elytra of a dark and shiny red color. They do not have metathoracic wings. The
head contains 6 ocelli, the antennae have four short segments and the mandibles are strongly
3-184
==================================================================================
PAGE 200
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
curved. The thorax has a reduced number of setae, well-developed and functional legs. And finally;
The abdomen bears a pair of long caudal appendages articulated in the last segment on each side of the
anal tube. In the case of the female, it can be differentiated by the shape of the pronotum, which is wider and
long, with a longitudinal line in the midline, a larger abdomen and more pointed elytra in the
apex. While males have keels or carinae on antennal segments 6, 7 and 8, their tarsi
The first pair of legs are widened and the apex of their elytra is rounded.
Its development cycle is approximately three months; females build galleries in the ground
to deposit 10 to 15 eggs that hatch in two weeks; These eggs have a length of
four millimeters and a maximum diameter of two at the widest pole, white and semi-cylindrical.
The larval stages are identified by the width of the head capsule and during the pupal phase.
It has strong pigmentation in the eyes, jaws and tibias; When it hatches from the pupa, it is found
discolored, developing its color in the following 24 hours.
In the field it can be found throughout the year, but in less dense activity in winter and its
Food consists of small insects, carrion and ripe wild fruits.
ii. Euathlus manicata
For this species, its current taxonomy is presented in Table 3-35.
Table 3-35 Taxonomy of Euathlus manicata
Kingdom Animalia Order Araneae
Division Arthropoda Family Theraphosidae
Class Arachnida Genus Euathlus
GAC Source
Euathlus manicata, tarantula or chick spider is a native species in Chile that is distributed in the
Regions of O'Higgins, Maule and Biobío. It has no quantitative background regarding its population, however
However, it is severely affected by the loss
dida and fragmentation of its habitat due to its low
mobility, limited dispersal mechanisms and sedentary habits; and for its extraction for trade
like exotic pets.
It is included in the Chilean Regulations D.S No. 05 of 1998 Regulations of the Hunting Law No. 4,601. (DTO -
133/93 Transitory Provisions, Art 4), replaced by Law No. 19,473; SAG criteria for
considering it a protected species are, according to Art 3 of the Hunting Law, being a 'species cataloged with
reduced population densities (S)' and be 'beneficial for the maintenance of the balance of
ecosystems (E)’. The classification committee concludes that its conservation category, according to the
Wild Species Classification Regulation (RCE) is Near Threatened (NT).

Adults of this species are distinguished by having black spiniform setae on the coxa.
prolateroventral and retrolateroventral in the maxilla. In juvenile specimens, sexual dimorphism is barely
noticeable, but in adults, females can be identified by the shape of their spermathecae, which
They are unilobed and form a semicircle with a wide base. These structures show a rough texture
from the middle portion to the apical area. On the other hand, males are distinguished by the morphology of
its palp organs, which are wide with accessory keels at the apex. Furthermore, its legs are
proportionally longer than those of females, and their abdomen is smaller.
Regarding coloration, females predominate in a greenish-brown tone, with a black abdomen.
decorated with gray and white mushrooms. At the base of the abdomen, they have a reddish tuft and a patch of
stinging setae in the dorsal area. The cephalothorax and femurs exhibit a yellowish green tone, while
that the rest of the legs are black with gray setae. For their part, the males share the same
coloration than females, although the tone of the cephalothorax is much brighter. The maximum size of
"Females reach 11-12 cm, while males are proportionally smaller."
Its diet is carnivorous and in terms of habitat, it prefers to make its burrow underground, although
It can also be found under rocks, logs or natural holes in the ground.

Phrixotrichus scrofa or coastal fox tarantula is a species native to Chile known only in some

unknown.

Morphologically, males are distinguished from P. jara by not having the serrated lower prolateral keel and

iv. Acanthogonathus pissi
For this species, its current taxonomy is presented in Table 3-37.

Acanthogonatus pissi or golden zebra spider is a species endemic to Chile, with a wide distribution
which goes from the Coquimbo Region to the Biobío Region. It is found in relatively
dry areas of the central zone, as well as in forested areas of the southern zone. They live under rocks and fallen logs,
where they make tubular fabrics that are frequently visible from the outside (Goloboff, 1995).
It is currently included in the Hunting Law No. 19,473 of 2015 and the Classification Committee concludes that
Its Conservation Category according to the Wild Species Classification Regulation (RCE) is
Least Concern (LC).
Morphologically they are recognized by having a reddish cephalothorax and black abdomen with oblique stripes.
yellowish and black legs and palps. Copulatory bulb of the male with the tip enlarged, unlike
other species of the genus. The female's hold is similar to that found with other species.
(Goloboff, 1995). Adult females have a beautiful yellowish-reddish coloration on the cephalothorax and
legs, while the abdomen is black with yellowish stripes. Adult males have tones
a little brighter and the legs are proportionally longer in relation to the body than in the case of
females and the abdomen proportionally smaller.

It feeds mainly on other arthropods, including members of its own species.
3.2.5.4 Susceptibility to the effects of Climate Change
In accordance with the criteria established in Resolution No. 202399101883/2023 dated 10
November 20232, of the Environmental Evaluation Service, the Project site area would be
affected by a reduction in accumulated annual precipitation. According to the P25 percentile
of the available climate projections, this reduction would reach a value of 20.1%, while
According to the P75th percentile of the projections, there would be a decrease of 9.5%. Regarding the
trends in temperatures, an increase in the average annual temperature is projected by a magnitude
of 1.4 °C throughout the continental national territory.
The wildlife component may be affected by future changes in precipitation patterns
and temperature. In accordance with what is indicated in Annex 1-11, the observations of the historical period
(1985-2019) show a significant trend of decrease in precipitation on an annual scale
at meteorological stations (Dichato, Carriel Sur and Cancha Los Litres stations). As for the
future projections of rainfall, these continue with negative trends, although not
All models present statistical significance for this result. Historical records give
in turn, there is a significant increase in days without rain in the autumn months in the stations
analyzed, a trend that is also observed for projections under a scenario of change
climate.
Regarding changes in temperature, the information available in Annex 1-11 shows
an increase in a set of maximum temperature indicators, with greater significance in summer
and on an annual scale. Increases in heat waves are projected, and longer days and nights are generally expected
warm weather and progressive increases in temperature, over t
heard in summer. This is expected to impact
an increase in the evapotranspiration demand of crops and increase the risk of fires
forestry. More background information available in Annex 1-11.
Changes in climate patterns are reflected in the increased risk of species loss
of fauna due to future changes in precipitation and temperature patterns, which could be reflected
in the risk of loss of fauna species as a result of changes in average annual precipitation
(Table 3-38) and in the average annual temperature (Table 3-39). At the same time, increases are projected in rates of
2 Evaluation Criterion in the SEIA: Climate change in the environmental evaluation of water resources.

3.2.6 Synthesis and Conclusions
The present study includes the characterization or baseline for invertebrate fauna (entomofauna)m
based on summer and winter 2023 campaigns, for the Project area. In this sense, through
of the sampling techniques proposed for the present study, it was possible to determine, through the two
campaigns, a total of 247 species. In this sense, in the 2023 summer campaign a total
of 109 species and 160 for the winter campaign within the Area of influence.
3-189
==================================================================================
PAGE 205
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Within the study area, it was determined to evaluate the entomofauna in 6 points during summer and 7 points
during winter of which present variability and representativeness in the environments of the area of
total influence of the project, achieving the registration and observation of 2,552 individuals, represented in a
effort of 54.5% according to the analysis of the species accumulation curve indices
during the summer and 67.6 during the winter campaign.
247 species were recorded in both campaigns, where 4 have conservation considerations.
in accordance with the resolution of classification of species in category by the Ministry of the Environment
(RCE) and the Hunting Law: Ceroglossus chilensis cyanicollis, Phrixotrichus scrofa, Euathlus manicata and
Acanthogonathus pissi.
The invertebrate community in the area of influence of the Project for the summer season is
made up of 3 classes of arthropods: Insecta, Arachnida and Entognatha. For its part, the campaign
winter, the presence of 6 classes was observed: Insecta, Arachnida, Entognatha, Diplopoda, Chilopoda and
Malacostraca. The Insecta class dominates the assemblage by 96% in summer and 71.5% in winter compared to
the other classes observed.
The Diversity of the community studied was determined as medium to high with a higher value of
3.11 in PME15 during summer and 3.37 in PME 5 during winter.
The variability of environments considered in each of the entomological sampling points was
significant and representative to all sectors without generating similarity of species, or assemblages that
can consider similarity according to the evaluated coefficients of both Bray-Curtis and
Jaccard.
