3.3 Continental Aquatic Ecosystems
3.3.1 Introduction
This report describes the physical, chemical and biological characteristics of Aquatic Ecosystems.
Continental areas of the Project's area of influence.
The environmental characterization was carried out in accordance with the regulatory requirements contained in the Law
N°19,300 on General Environmental Bases and within the framework of what is established in letter e.2) of the
Article 18 of the D.S. N°40 of 2012, modified by D.S. N°30 of 2023, both from the Ministry of the Environment
Environment, Regulation of the Environmental Impact Assessment System (RSEIA).
Below are the results of the autumn, winter and spring seasonal campaigns.
of 2022 and summer, autumn and winter 2023, for the physicochemical environmental quality characteristics of the
water associated with the Chilean Standard 1,333 and the continental aquatic communities present in the
area of influence of the Project in its winter 2022, summer and spring 2023 campaigns.
3.3.2 Objectives
3.3.2.1 General objective
Describe and quantify the composition and abundance of aquatic biota and evaluate the chemical and physical quality
of continental waters and sediments of continental waters in the area of influence of the
Project.
3.3.2.2 Specific objectives
• Describe and quantify the chemical, physical and microbiological composition of the water column and the
sediments.
• Describe and quantify the composition and abundance of aquatic biota.
3.3.3 Area of influence
The general criteria for determining the area of influence are derived from the RSEIA, the Guides
issued by the SEA and, in particular, the elements indicated in the guide “Description of the components
soil, flora and fauna of terrestrial ecosystems in the SEIA (SEA, 2015). According to the above, the
Determination and justification of the area of influence was based on the following criteria:
Criterion 1: Hydrographic network where the Project works and activities will be located.
3-193
==================================================================================
PAGE 209
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Criterion 2: Specific physical space in a river, estuary, stream or any continental body of water, in
where works and activities of the Project alter the elements that make it up (physicochemical attributes,
sediments, habitat of aquatic biota), to the extent that this can be evaluated.
Criterion 3: Applicable regulations and reference thresholds, such as NCh 1,333 that defines the elements
irrigation water maximums, water requirements for direct contact recreation, and water requirements
general water conditions for aquatic life. For what is not covered by current regulations, you must
use reference ranges as indicated in Article 11 of the RSEIA.
Based on the criteria indicated above, the area of influence for the ecosystem component
continental aquatic corresponds to the geographic space
fico, defined by 200 meters upstream and 200 m
downstream, where the Project interacts with continental watercourses due to the development of its
works, parts and activities, in which the hydrographic system could be affected in a localized manner, such as
as displayed in Figure 3-114. Annex 3B-46 presents the AI ​​in digital format.

3.3.4.2 Characterization of Physical Parameters
Because some water quality parameters rapidly undergo variations in their
composition, it is necessary to determine them in situ at the time they are monitored. These parameters
were: Temperature, pH, Conductivity and Dissolved Oxygen, which were measured using a probe
multiparameter HANNA INSTRUMENTS model HI9829, following its instructions for use, maintenance and
verification, recording all values on a land form. The characterization of the substrate
each station was carried out according to the methodology described by Bain et al. (1985), developed for
Habitat analysis of aquatic organisms. The assigned codes are described in Table 3-44 below.
to the size classes of the different types of substrate.

Obtaining samples and preserving water and sediment quality parameters is

3.3.4.4 Biological Parameters
i. Ichthyofauna
Sampling was carried out using a portable electric fishing equipment, model SAMUS-725G. The
regulation of the voltage and/or amperage of the equipment was determined in the field, according to the conductivity
of the water and the target species of fish present in the sector. For example, when the
presence of siluriform species, the frequency and amperage were decreased, to avoid mortality due to
fishing, given that these organisms have a naked body (without the presence of scales), and are more
sensitive to the electric field. On the other hand, when quantifying the presence only of salmoniform species, the
frequency and voltage was increased, given that these organisms have a greater swimming capacity and
They can escape the electric field generated by the equipment.
All individuals were collected in 100 m transects for each monitoring station, for a
time of approximately 30 minutes, depending on the morphological conditions of each
season (depth, vegetation cover and water speed in the channel among others). The activity,
It was developed by 2 professionals, where one carried the fishing equipment, as well as a hand net,
while the other professional also had a hand net and was also carrying a bucket with
fresh water, where the captured organisms were retained. Abundance was expressed as
Capture per Unit of Effort (CPUE) expressed as Individuals captured in a time of 30
minutes. All specimens collected were identified in situ to the taxonomic level of species.
following the references indicated by Habit et al. 2006. In addition, the length
total (TL) and total weight (TP) of the captured individuals, to later release them in the same
station where they were obtained. The measurements of total length and weight also allowed the
estimation of the Condition Factor (Kc) or robustness of the fish according to the following formula:
Kc = (PT/LT3) *100 (Lagler 1956)
The conservation status of the aquatic fauna species present in the location area is
determined in accordance with the official lists of species with conservation problems for the southern zone
of Chile (CONAMA 2009; D.S: N° 5/98), Technical Report (R. PESQ.) N° 48/2011 Extractive ban for fauna
Threatened native fish in continental waters and D.S. No. 151/07, D.S. N° 50/08, D.S.N°51/08, D.S.
N°23/09, D.S N° 33/11, D.S. N°41/12, D.S. N°42/12, all from MINSEGPRES, and D.S. N°19/12 and D.S.
N°13/2013, all from the Ministry of the Environment; that make official the first to the last process of
classification of species respectively, dictated as established in the Classification Regulations
3-201
==================================================================================
PAGE 217
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
of Wild Species (D.S. No. 75 of the MINSEGPRES of 2006, replaced by D.S. No. 29 of the MMA of
2011).
ii. Phytobenthos
At each sampling site, 3 replicas of phytobenthos were collected, with the help of a bristle brush.
softly he scraped his face exposed to the water column from some boulder or stone submerged in the channel. The
area to sample (scraping), corresponded to 20 cm2. What was obtained through scraping was deposited
in jars with a slight amount of water, and then fixed with a Lugol's solution for later
identification of taxa in the laboratory. Furthermore, and to analyze the estimated biomass as
concentration of Chlorophyll-a, 2 samples of the entire periphyton of the scraped area were saved in tubes
Falcon covered in aluminum foil and preserved in cold (thermo-insulated boxes with units
frozen refrigerants) and then, using extraction techniques in cold 90% acetone, read the
lengths of o
nda of the supernatant in a Turner Designs model 10.05R fluorometer. The
phytobenthic assemblage was characterized based on the average density of 3 replicates (cel/cm2) and richness
total (number of taxa) (Krebs 1989). The classification of microalgae followed the work of Simonsen
(1987), Round et al. (1996), Rumrich et al. (2000).
iii. Macroinvertebrates
To characterize the benthic macroinvertebrate community, 3 replicates were obtained in
each station sampled, from an area with primarily hard substrate, using a network type
“Surber” with an area of 0.09 m2 and a mesh aperture of 200 μm. This network was submerged to the bottom of the
river (average depth 25 cm) against the current. The stones and/or balls associated with the area of
sampling of the net, they are removed slightly with the hands, to release the attached macroinvertebrates
to this type of substrate. In this way, the organisms are dragged by the current and trapped in the
network mesh. Subsequently, the samples were preserved in a solution with 70% alcohol and
transferred to the laboratory, where each sample was cleaned (extraction of excess sediment, leaves,
branches) to then identify all the individuals present, with the help of a Microscope
stereoscopic with cold light, with magnification from 0.7x to 4.5x. The classification of organisms was made based on
to the works of Bertrand (1995), Lopretto and Tell (1995), Lugo-Ortiz and McCafferty (1999a, 1999b) and Merrit
and Cummins (1996). The assembly was characterized by the parameters average density (ind/m2),
total richness (number of taxa) (Krebs 1989).
iv. Phytoplankton
Phytoplankton was obtained by collecting 3 replicates of the filtrate of 10 L of water in a net of
25 microns of weft for quantitative sampling, while for qualitative counting the haul was used
of the 25 micron phytoplankton net in a horizontal position for 10 minutes against the current.
Each sample, collected at each sampling station, was fixed with Lugol's reagent. The count
3-202
==================================================================================
PAGE 218
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Quantitative was carried out in the laboratory according to the Utermöhl methodology. A microscope was used
inverted Motic brand model A31 equipped with phase contrast and interference. For identification
taxonomic, the works of Parra et al. were used. (1982-83), Simonsen (1987), Rivera (1983), Round et
al. (1996), Rumrich et al. (2000) and Lange-Bertalot and Krammer (1989). The results are expressed in number
of cells per liter (No. cells/L).
v. Zooplankton
Zooplankton was obtained by filtering 20 liters of water through a net with a 70 µm opening.
mesh. The samples were fixed in situ in 70% alcohol for preservation and subsequent analysis of
laboratory. Quantitative counting in the laboratory was carried out using a stereoscopic microscope.
with the samples arranged in Bogorov chambers and using mechanical counters. For identification
taxonomic, the works of Araya and Zúñiga (1985) and Pennak (1989) were used. The results were expressed
in number of individuals per liter (No. Ind/L).
3.3.5 Results
3.3.5.1 Water quality
i. Fall 2022 campaign
a) Physical Parameters
During the field campaign, the temperature of the water column fluctuated between 7.3°C (season
P10) and 10.1°C (station P12). For pH, the lowest concentration was recorded at station P23 where
a concentration of 7.1 pH units was recorded, while in P19 a concentration
of 8.4 pH units. The recording of the Dissolved Oxygen concentration varied between 6.2 mg/L in the
station P23 and 8.9 mg/L at station P14. Finally, the Electrical conductivity, the concentrations
They fluctuated between 71 S/cm at station P7 and 201 S/cm at station P13 (Table 3-48).

b) Relationship with Chilean Standard No. 1333
The temperature values recorded in the study area were within the limits
established NCh. 1.333 (water requirements for direct contact recreation, set forth in Table
N°3) where a maximum value of 30°C is established for waters that can be used with direct contact.
In the case of the concentration of dissolved Oxygen, the same standard in Table No. 4 where they are delivered
The general requirements for water intended for aquatic life establishes a minimum value of 5 as a limit.
mg/L. Based on the above, the four stations sampled during this campaign would comply with the
requirement established in said standard. The pH values in this campaign comply with the limits
established from Table No. 4 of NCh 1,333, which establishes as a requirement a range from 6.0
up to 9.0 pH units.
In accordance with the provisions of Table No. 2 of NCh 1,333 (Classification of water for irrigation according to its
salinity), the electrical conductivity concentration for the four sampling stations was
classified as “Water with which generally no harmful effects will be observed”, given that the
Concentrations quantified at these sampling stations were less than 750 S/cm.
c) Macroelements
In relation to the macroelements quantified at the sampling stations, it was possible to establish
that all the indicated parameters comply with the l
limits established in the reference regulations,
exception of the Percent Sodium concentration that at stations P7, P10, P12, P18, P19 and P22
exceeded the regulated limit of 35%. For Fluoride and Cyanide, values were recorded below the limit
analytical detection at all sampled stations (Table 3-57).
d) Inorganic elements
The spatial variability of the concentration of Ammonia, Ammonium and Ammoniacal Nitrogen is described.
where stations P13 and P23 stood out for presenting the highest values with 4.53, 4.82 and 3.76 mg/L
respectively. These elements are not included in Chilean Standard No. 1333 (Table 3-57).
For more details of the results, see Appendix 1-2. Laboratory Certificates.

In accordance with the provisions of Table No. 2 of NCh 1,333 (Classification of water for irrigation according to its
salinity), the electrical conductivity concentration for the four sampling stations was
classified as “Water with which generally no harmful effects will be observed”, given that the
Concentrations quantified at these sampling stations were less than 750 S/cm.
c) Macroelements
In relation to the macroelements quantified at the sampling stations, it was possible to establish
that all the indicated parameters comply with the limits established in the reference regulations, to
exception of the Percent Sodium concentration that in all stations the regulated limit was exceeded
(Table 3-65). For Cyanide, values were recorded below the analytical detection limit in all
the sampled stations. While the Sulfate was concentrated above the limit of the norm in the
only in station P9 with 1,7722.4 mg/L. For more details of the results, see Annex 3B-45.
d) Inorganic elements
The spatial variability of the concentration of Ammonia (Ammoniacal Nitrogen) is described, where
Stations P13 stood out, reaching the highest value of 9.3 mg/L respectively. These elements do not
They are included in Chilean Standard No. 1333 (Table 3-65). For more details of the results, see Annex
3B-45.

3.3.5.3 Physicochemical parameters of water, sediments and aquatic biota
i. Winter campaign 2022
a) In situ water quality
The parameters quantified in situ describe the water channel at each sampling station, where
indicate that these are bodies of water that comply with the provisions of NCh 1,333 for the
concentrations of pH, dissolved oxygen and temperature. At the same time, they are described as bodies of
shallow waters with a maximum depth of 23.6 cm in P9 and a minimum of 7.1 cm in P23, as well as the
Channel velocity varied between 0.2 m/s at stations P17 and P23 to a maximum of 0.9 m/s at P7 (Table
3-76).

a) In situ water quality and channel characteristics

indicates that these are bodies of water that meet the thresholds of NCh 1,333 for the

shallow waters with a maximum depth of 22.48 cm in P10 and a minimum of 16.65 cm in P7, in turn the
Channel speed varied between 0.2 m/s at stations PE, P14 and P17 and a maximum of 0.53 m/s at P9,
while the width of the channel varied between 1.76 m at station P1 and 6 m at station P7 (Table 3-104).

3.3.5.4 Relationship between climate change and Continental Aquatic Ecosystems in
the area of influence of the project
In accordance with the criteria established in Resolution No. 202399101883/2023 dated 10
November 20233, of the Environmental Evaluation Service, the Project site area would be
affected by a reduction in accumulated annual precipitation. According to the P25 percentile
of the available climate projections, this reduction would reach a value of 20.1%, while
According to the P75th percentile of the projections, there would be a decrease of 9.5%. Regarding the
trends in temperatures, an increase in the average annual temperature is projected by a magnitude
of 1.4 °C throughout the continental national territory.
Consequently, for Continental Aquatic Ecosystems the main climate risks in the
commune of Penco are related to both the decrease in rainfall and the increase in
the temperatures. A strong increase in the risk of hydrological drought stands out between the historical period
(1980-2010) and a future period (2035-2065). Similarly, a sharp increase in risk is projected
on rural and urban domestic water security between the historical period and the future period in the
Penco commune.
In the case of health risk due to floods that could cause rivers to overflow, there is no
There is information available for the Penco commune, however, the risk of flooding of
coastal sectors due to storm surges and rising sea levels are evaluated at a high level in the locality
from Penco.
In relation to the increase in temperatures, a slight increase in the temperature is projected in the commune of Penco.
risk of adverse effects on the health of the population due to an increase in heat waves and the risk
of premature mortality due to heat. The risk of fires in urban settlements, which is seen
accentuated by the occurrence of temperatures above 30 °C, it is evaluated with a low increase between the
historical period and the future period in the commune of Penco, as indicated and detailed in Annex 1-12
Climate risk assessment.
3.3.6 Synthesis and conclusions
For the chemical quality of the surface water, it was possible to indicate that the quality of the sampled waters
complies with the requirements considered in Chilean Standard No. 1,333, with the exception of the parameter
Percent Sodium, which showed that its concentrations were higher than the threshold established by
the aforementioned standard; this in the majority of the stations sampled in the campaigns carried out.
The Total Organic Matter in sediments present from the sampling stations, reported that during
In the 2022 winter campaign, the highest concentration was quantified in station P10 with 8.377%.
3 Evaluation Criterion in the SEIA: Climate change in the environmental evaluation of water resources.
3-297
==================================================================================
PAGE 313
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
while at station P1 the lowest percentage of 0.266% was recorded. However, during the campaign
of summer 2023 reported that the highest concentration was quantified in station P1 with 2.72%, while
that
At station P7 the lowest percentage was recorded with 0.81%.
The presence of ichthyofauna was detected, which was associated with the bodies of water that presented a
greater surface runoff and correspond only to those located in the Penco estuary (PE,
P7, P9 and P10), where the presence of the native species T. areolatus was recorded, present in the campaigns
sampling.
In relation to the phytoplankton (water column) and phytobenthonic (substrate) communities, the
described species correspond to taxa typical of continental water systems. Likewise,
The quantified abundances indicate environments with a trophic state that supports productivity.
primary of the aquatic system described allowing the occurrence of other taxa characteristic of the
continental aquatic ecosystems.
The zoobenthos (macroinvertebrates) recorded the presence of early indicator taxa of
pollution (family Chironomidae and Simulidae) (Figueroa et al. 2003). The family Chironomidae was recorded
in all the sampled stations, being also the most abundant. However, given the wide
range of environmental conditions in which it is possible to find this type of organism (Medina & Paggi
2004), its sole presence would not necessarily be an indicator of water alteration, a situation that is
supported by the fact that the rest of the sampling stations also recorded the presence of
individuals belonging to taxa recognized as indicators of good quality water, such as
Ephemeroptera and Odonata (Figueroa et al. 2003).

3.4 Ecosystem Relationships
3.4.1 Introduction
In accordance with the provisions of literal e.2) of article 18 of the D.S. N°40/12 of the Ministry of the Environment
Environment (MMA), the projects and/or activities submitted to environmental evaluation through a Study
Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA) must account for the background that characterizes the Relationships
Ecosystems. The aforementioned literal indicates, e.2 that
“Terrestrial ecosystems, which will include both a description and analysis of soil, plants, algae, fungi and
wild animals, as well as other biotic elements. This description will include, among others, the
identification, location, distribution, diversity and abundance of the species that make up the
existing ecosystems, identifying those species that are found in some category of
conservation in accordance with the provisions of article 37 of the Law. Likewise, the relationships
existing with the physical environment and with continental and marine aquatic ecosystems.”
This document gives an account of the relationships between the physical environment and ecosystems
terrestrial and aquatic present in the Area of Influence (AI) of the “Mining Development Project of
Extraction of Clays for the Production of Rare Earth Concentrates” (hereinafter, the Project),
located in the communes of Penco and Concepción, Biobío Region. According to the above, in the
This section characterizes the relevant ecosystem relationships between the physical environment
(abiotic component) and terrestrial and aquatic ecosystems (biotic component).
In functional terms, an ecosystem corresponds to an area where organisms interact with each other.
them and with the physical environment. These interactions include basic ecosystem processes such as the flow of
energy and nutrients at different hierarchical levels (Whitford, 2002). Based on the preceding definition,
It is then understood that the relationships of the ecosystem will be determined by climate, geography,
structure of the vegetation, the faunal assemblage (vertebrates and invertebrates) and the interventions
existing anthropic; All of them will determine the form of relationship, connection and the flow of energy and
nutrients in the area of influence of the Project, defined for the biotic and abiotic components
studied.
To analyze the ecosystem relationships, the environmental characteristics of the AI of the
Project, taking into account the results of the Baselines obtained. In particular, they present
the existing relationships between the biological components and the physical environment that are part of the
ecosystems present in the Project AI, as well as the relationships between the different types of
ecosystems.
3-300
==================================================================================
PAGE 316
==================================================================================

Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production

3.4.1.1 Background
An "ecosystem" refers to the set of species within a specific area that interact with each other and
with its abiotic environment. These interactions include processes such as predation, parasitism,
competition and symbiosis. Additionally, organisms contribute to the ecosystem by decomposing and
reintegrate into the energy and nutrient cycle. The species that make up an ecosystem, among which
They include bacteria, fungi, plants and animals, maintain fundamental interdependencies with each other.
These relationships, together with their environment, result in flows of matter and energy within the ecosystem. This
is displayed
as an open system that encompasses both living communities and elements
abiotics. The biotic components (such as flora and fauna) and abiotic components (such as the conditions
climatic, chemical and physical factors) maintain a dynamic balance. This balance, which entails
certain stability, a defined structure and a characteristic appearance, is the result of a long
and continuous process that is summarized in three properties: structure, organization and homeostasis. These
properties are the product of a long and continuous process, in which different organisms arrange themselves
in a system, according to its ecological capabilities, until stability is achieved in relation to the
characteristics of the soil, climate and topography of the site, among other environmental factors (Fournier, 2003).
Ecosystems have two main biotic components. The first is an autotrophic component
(self-powered), capable of fixing light energy and producing food from inorganic substances
simple (such as water, carbon dioxide and nitrates) through the process of photosynthesis. Generally,
Green plants, whether terrestrial vegetation or algae and aquatic plants, constitute the component
autotroph. These organisms are considered the producers. The second main unit is the component
heterotroph (that feeds on others), which uses, redistributes and decomposes complex materials
synthesized by autotrophs. Fungi, non-photosynthetic bacteria and other microorganisms, as well as
animals, including humans, constitute heterotrophs, which concentrate their activities in the
soil and sediments under the canopy (Odum & Sarmiento, 1998). These organisms are considered the
consumers, since they cannot produce their own food, and must obtain it by consuming other
organisms. We can subdivide heterotrophs based on the source of their food energy. So,
we have herbivores or prey, which feed on plants; carnivores or predators, which
they feed on other animals; the superior carnivores or predators, which feed on
predators; omnivores, which consume both plants and animals; and saprophages (mainly
fungi and bacteria), which feed on decaying organic matter (Figure 3-131) (Odum &
Sarmiento, 1998).
3-301
==================================================================================
PAGE 317
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Figure 3-131. Terrestrial and aquatic ecosystem model based on nutritional energy exchange
I. Abiotic components (essential inorganic and organic compounds); II. Producers (vegetation on land and phytoplankton in
water); III. Macroconsumers or animals: A. direct herbivores or browsers (insects, rodents on land and zooplankton
in the water); B. indirect consumers (detrithophages or saprovores, soil invertebrates on land and benthic invertebrates
in the water); C. higher carnivores (raptors and large fish); IV. Decomposers (bacteria and fungi).
Source: Odum & Sarmiento, 1998

3.4.4.3 Ecosystem relationships
It must be considered that individuals, populations and communities are in a network of interactions
between organisms and the flows of energy and nutrients. These interactions are explained with the application
of theories related to ecological succession, food webs, biodiversity and
ecosystem functioning.
Based on the above, this document analyzes the trophic networks or webs (source of energy
used) and the processes that define or result from them, taking into account the results obtained in
the different baselines.
Thus, the components of the ecosystems were defined based on the following criteria:
- Environments
- Dominant species
- Key species
- Functional groups
- Abiotic elements
- Flows
matter and energy

They established the relationships between them through conceptual flow diagrams of matter and energy.
direct and indirect effects possible to identify between biotic and abiotic components. As a basis of the
models are the producers who sustain the flow of the chain and where they are concentrated
mainly the contribution of abiotic elements to the system such as water, minerals and fixation of
nutrients in the soil.

3.4.4.4 Forcing factors
Forcing factors are those that affect or regulate the global functioning of ecosystems. Yes
They may well be of natural or anthropic origin, by definition they are external to the ecosystem under study (SAG,
2006). Anthropic forcing factors are considered to be related to the occupation of the territory and the
changes in land use, while natural forcing factors correspond mainly to
availability of water (both surface and underground aquifers), the physical-chemical quality of the water,
eutrophication, erosion and soil quality (main substrate), non-native species, forest fires and
climate change (the latter as a mix between anthropic and natural).
The main forcing factors of each ecosystem were identified from the descriptions of the
ecosystems and their relationships.
3.4.5 Results
The most relevant properties of the abiotic and biotic components are described below.
characterized in the background and baselines of the Project.
3.4.5.1 General description of the abiotic factors of the area of influence
i. Climate and geomorphology
According to the Köppen classification, the Project is located in the zone designated as "Temperate
warm with winter rains and high atmospheric humidity", which is part of type C climates: Climates
tempered. These climates are characterized by having average temperatures of the coldest month between -3°C and 18°C,
accompanied by seasonal precipitation. Furthermore, it is in these regions where the
temperate forests. Temperate climates are typical of the western coasts of the continents,
generally situated between latitudes 30° and 40°. Examples of these areas include central Chile,
the central coast of California, the Cape region of South Africa, and the mountainous interior areas of the
Iberian Peninsula, especially above 900 or 1,000 meters above sea level.
Specifically, the climate classification "Warm temperate with winter rains and high atmospheric humidity"
It is characterized by a dry summer with a marked lack of precipitation: the amount of rain for the month
driest summer is less than a third of the precipitation of the wettest month, and in some months
precipitation is less than 30 mm. Furthermore, summer is cool, since the temperature
average of the warmest month does not exceed 22 °C. For at least four months of the year, temperatures
averages exceed 10 °C. This type of climate also experiences rare fogs, with high humidity in the
air and little rain, all accompanied by relatively low temperatures (summers with less than
24°C).

==================================================================================
PAGE 322
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Since the Köppen climate classification provides a description of the climate on a spatial scale
broad, it is necessary to carry out a characterization of the local climate for the Project area. According to the Atlas
Agroclimatic of Chile (AGRIMED Center, 2017), the project is located in the district
agroclimatic 8-9-1 of Concepción, classified as Suprathermal warm temperate with a regime of
dry subhumid humidity. This district is characterized by having temperatures that range between
average maximum of 22.5 °C in January (with maximum of 26 °C and minimum
s of 18.6 °C within the district) and
a minimum of 5.7°C in July (with highs of 6.5°C and lows of 4.7°C within the district). Furthermore,
It has an average of 297 consecutive days without frost per year, with a total of 3 frosts recorded
annually. The favorable period for vegetative activity extends for 9 months, while
1,185 degree days and 443 hours of cold have accumulated until July 31. The average annual rainfall is
1,135 mm, with a dry period of 5 months and a water deficit of 465 mm per year. On the other hand, the period
humid covers 5 months, during which a water surplus of 464 mm is recorded.
The area where the Project works will be carried out is located within the sub-basins of the River
Andalién and the coastal areas that extend from the Pingueral River to the Andalién River. These areas
They border the commune of Penco and are part of the basin that includes the coastal areas and islands between the
Itata River and the Biobío River. Precipitation in the area is concentrated mainly in the winter months,
with the exception of a sampling station in the AI, where the precipitation during the year 2020 reached
its maximum in summer months.
Regarding geology, rocks from the Coastal Batholith of Southern Chile are found in the Biobío Region.
(Hervé et al., 1987) of upper Carboniferous age (Deckart et al., 2014), distributed in a strip of
direction NNE-SSW. At their eastern limit, these rocks come into contact with the Metamorphic Complex
Paleozoic accretionary (Creixell, 2001). In the coastal area of the region, rocks can be found
Cretaceous sedimentary rocks of the Quiriquina Formation (Biró, 1982) and the Paleocene-Eocene Formation
Curanilahue, which consist of marine and marine-continental sequences. In addition, there are other sequences
sedimentary areas close to the project area, such as sediments and marine terraces. The specific area of
Project is located on the Coastal Batholith. Three intrusive igneous units are defined: Granite of
Garnet, Diorite and Biotite Granite, which are grouped in the “Penco Plutonic Complex”.
In relation to geomorphology, at a regional level in the vicinity of the place where the
Project, the relief is characterized by its complexity, presenting a variety of slopes that range
from flat areas to sectors with abrupt changes. Near the coast, steeper slopes predominate.
gentle, while to the west, in the Coastal Mountains, the slopes become steeper.
pronounced. Within the area of influence of the Project, modeling by slope ranges reveals
the complexity of the relief, with a variety of slopes. River water course modeling
intermittent and perennial are the main responsible for geomorphological modeling, highlighting the
incision of the Estero Penco.

ii. Terrestrial Ecosystems
d) Soils
The Project is located in the edaphic area called “Arid Mediterranean Zones” (Luzio, 2010),
specifically in the soil area of Cerros de la Cordillera de la Costa. On these soils, the relief is
pronounced, with slopes that vary from 5% to 8% and from 8% to 15% in the hillside sectors; from 30 to
50% and more than 50% in the hill areas themselves. The parent material corresponds to rock
metamorphic, with different degrees of weathering, but with its recognizable schistose structure that
It still manifests itself in the lower horizons of the soils. Fine and very fine textural classes,
generally with an increase in the fine fraction towards the lower horizons. The depth of the
soils is generally greater than one meter, the structures are made of strong medium subangular blocks and
the distribution of fine and medium roots is common to abundant in contact with the underlying rock.

The agrological study indicates that the Project is specifically based on two types of associations,
Constitución (KT) variation KT-5, and Treguaco (TG) with variations TG-3 and TG-4. The first presents a
deep, well-evolved, residual soil, formed from metamorphic rocks, especially
slates, has a sandy clay loam texture on the surface and silty clay in depth. If
is deposited on a substrate made up of highly weathered metamorphic rocks with
clay matrix that allows the development of deep roots. On the other hand, the Treguaco association is a
deep, well-evolved soil formed from metamorphic rocks, especially micasites and
highly weathered gneiss; silty clay loam texture throughout the profile. Rests on a substrate
Consisting of highly weathered metamorphic rocks with high content of mica and quartz. They are floors
Well structured, friable, with good porosity that allow good root development. Both
Associations have an assigned CCUS of VII, mainly due to slope limitations.
The Potential erosion risk for the AI indicates that 56.7% of the surface is classified as
“Severe”, while 35% are defined as “moderate” class. The above is mainly due to the
pronounced slopes in the study area (CIREN, 2010).
Three homogeneous units of soil, hill, ravine and intervened are described. Cerro is the largest unit
representative of the AI (207.22 ha = 87.48%). It is characterized by developing in hilly sectors, with
pronounced slopes, with the characteristic of presenting a surface horizon of brighter colors
dark due to the presence of organic matter. Clay levels on the surface are presented
lower when compared with the lower horizons, showing textures between clay loam and loam.
on surface. The presence of roots can be observed throughout the profile, mainly in the horizons
superficial, in sizes ranging from fine, very fine

usable, in general they present fair to good, the above is associated with the fact that the soils of the area

The ravine corresponds to a narrow “V”-shaped opening located between the slopes of the hills.

complex from hilly to mountainous, with poor to imperfect drainage, with fine loamy to loamy textures
clayey. In general, they have regular usable humidity. Finally, the unit intervened

mainly.

8 to <15%, which occupies 16% of the area, equivalent to 37.91 ha. To a lesser extent, the ranges are identified
slopes less than 8% and greater than 45%.

iii. Aquatic Ecosystems
a) Physical-chemical parameters
Most of the sampling stations in which the parameters were analyzed are characterized by
present surface runoff of water, with small falls forming pools of different
dimensions, and an irregular bedrock accompanied by balls of different sizes with substrates that
They range from clay to fine, medium and very coarse sand. Regarding the quality of the sediments,
obtained varied percentages in the concentration of organic matter, presenting the concentration
highest during the winter 2022 campaign with 8.4%. In the sampled stations it varies
mainly the depth, with the deepest point being P1 with 53 cm in the campaign.
summer 2023, and the shallowest one is P23 with 7.1 cm in the winter 2022 campaign.
The speed of runoff in the channel also varies, being slower and shallower in some points such as
the P17, P23 and P14 in the winter 2022, summer 2023 and spring 2023 campaigns with values of 0.2 m/s,
or being faster with a maximum value in the P7 of 0.9 m/s during the 2022 winter campaign.
On the other hand, generally in the wider pools there are larger balls and in those points
where the runoff is more superficial and shallow there is greater vegetation coverage that can be
shrubby or compound. The above being the characteristics of the habitat of the biological components of the
aquatic system described in the Project.
Regarding water quality, it is important to evaluate the physicochemical characterization in parallel to the biota.
aquatic, since organisms such as aquatic macroinvertebrates are very sensitive to alterations
of the environment, which positions them as an indicator model of water quality (Forero et al., 2013). The use
of these organisms as indicators of water quality is based on the fact that they occupy a habitat
whose environmental demands are adapted. Any change in environmental conditions is
will reflect on the structures of the communities that live there (Forero et al., 2013), so it will also
These physicochemical conditions and habitat characteristics described above can determine
ar
the presence of biological components and how they relate to each other.
Taking into account the above, values of temperature in the water column, pH, oxygen
dissolved and electrical conductivity that met the requirements established in Chilean Standard No.
1,333.

manganese and iron are common, followed by arsenic in the summer and autumn campaigns of 2023. Without
However, it is worth noting that in the 2023 summer campaign the concentrations of the
greater amount of metals compared to the other campaigns, in addition to presenting the values of
highest concentration for each metal, highlighting aluminum with 162 mg/L, iron with 26.91 mg/L
and manganese with 3.76 mg/L. On the other hand, elements relevant to the development of microorganisms
how the BDO5 exceeded the norm in the winter and autumn 2023 campaigns in the same season
sampling (P23) with the highest concentration value of all campaigns, this being 102.3 mg/L.
3.4.5.2 General description of the biotic factors of the area of influence
i. Terrestrial ecosystems
a) Plants
Vegetation: The area of influence for the flora and vegetation component covers an area of 673.62
ha, which is composed of three environments: intervened, modified and natural. The environment
that has the greatest representation within the AI is the one intervened, reaching 71% of the total
(corresponding to 478.44 ha), with plantation being the dominant land use (370.38 ha), followed by
the wild forests (106.21 ha) and to a lesser extent the prairie (1.85 ha). They correspond to sectors
where the vegetation, although it presents natural elements and arises spontaneously, is shown
strongly influenced by actions of anthropic origin that have noticeably modified the
composition and structure of the natural plant community. In this way, the intervened environments
They concern areas where natural vegetation has been replaced by other vegetation cover,
mainly for forestry use. Regarding land use, the plantation is made up of two
extensive vegetation formations dominated by the exotic species Eucalyptus globulus and Pinus
radiata. Meanwhile, the wild forest is composed of four formations dominated
by the exotic species Acacia dealbata, Acacia melanoxylon, Eucalyptus globulus and Pinus radiata, and,
3-312
==================================================================================
PAGE 328
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
finally, the meadow composed of two vegetation formations dominated by grasses
introduced Holcus lanatus and Senecio vulgaris.
The natural environment continues, which covers 121.02 ha, equivalent to 18% of the total surface of the AI.
Within this environment, the presence of forest, scrub and grassland land uses stands out with
41.84 ha, 67.39 ha and 11.79 ha respectively. They correspond to those sectors where the vegetation
presents composition and structure characteristics close to natural conditions and where the effect
of anthropogenic activity has not generated noticeable changes in the physiognomy of the formations. It is
It is important to consider that forest type use includes native forest formations and native forest of
preservation (regulated by Law No. 20,283). Regarding land use, the forest is represented by
two sub-uses, one called native forest composed of five vegetation formations
dominated by the species Aextoxicon punctatum, Aristotelia chilensis, Laurelia sempervirens,
Nothofagus obliqua and Peumus boldus, and the second sub-use called native forest of
preservation also represented by five formations dominated by Aextoxicon species
punctatum, Aristotelia chilensis, Cryptocarya alba, Luma checken and Nothofagus obliqua. For the case of
under-use native preservation forest, this condition is given by the presence of the species in
conservation category Citronella mucronata (Vulnerable), Gomortega keule (Endangered) and Pitavia
punctata (Endangered). On the other hand, the bush type use is represented by two sub-uses, one
called scrub and the other arborescent scrub (in this the species of arboreal growth
play a more dominant role). Both are composed of three vegetation formations
dominated by the native shrub Fuchsia magellanica and the introduced shrubs Rubus ulmifolius and Teline
monspessulana. Finally, the grassland is represented by two vegetation formations dominated
by the native perennial herb Chusquea quila and the introduced perennial herbs Typha angustifolia and Lotus
pedunculatus.
Finally, the modified environment corresponds to the one with the least representativeness with 11% (74.16 ha),
which is exclusively composed of the use of urban and industrial areas. Mentions sectors
where the natural vegetation has been completely and artificially eradicated, also modifying the
characteristics of the soil as a biological entity, its productivity being eliminated.
Regarding the vegetation formations, the Eucalyptus globulus plantation, the wild forest
of Pinus radiata, the Teline monspessulana thicket and the Pinus radiata plantation are those that
predominate within the area of influence, with an area of 332.73 ha, 47.60 ha, 39.28 ha and 37.65 ha
respectively. For its part, the dominant native forest formations correspond to the forests
preservation natives of Nothofagus obliqua, Aextoxicon punctatum and Cryptocarya alba.
In general terms, it can be stated that the Project IA is composed mainly of
forest plantations and wild forests, followed by scrublands and forest formations.

Flora: 247 species of vascular plants were registered in the AI. The entire flora identified in the
The area of influence of the Project represents 4.6% of Chile's flora, according to the catalog of
Rodríguez and Marticorena (2019). 93.5% corresponds to the Magnoliophyta Division (231 species), within
of which the Class Magnoliopsida groups the majority of the taxa with 78.5% (194 species) and 15%
remaining correspond to the Class Liliopsida (37 species). Then the Pteridophyta Division continues with 14
species (5.7%), distributed mainly in Class Polypodiopsida (13 species). Finally, for the
Pinophyta Division, two species (0.8%) classified within the Pinopsida Class were recorded. The family
dominant corresponds to Asteraceae with 35 species, which is equivalent to 14.2% of the total record. It follows
the Fabaceae family with 13 species, Myrtaceae with 12, Poaceae with 11, and then the families continue
Lamiaceae and Rosaceae with nine records each. It is important to point out the importance at the level
ecosystem
ico of the species of the Fabaceae family (legumes), since most have
morphophysiological adaptations to water stress and the capacity for biological nitrogen fixation,
which makes them promising ecosystem species by improving the structure and quality of the soil
(Bianco & Cenzano, 2018).
The analysis of the flora according to phytogeographic origin reveals that species of native origin dominate with
40.9% (101 species), followed by introduced species (32.4%, 80 species) and in third place
endemic species of the country (25.9%, 64 species). Of the recorded flora, herbs dominate (annual and
perennial) with 58.7% (145 species), followed by shrubs with 26.3% (65 species) and trees
with 15% (37 species).
19 species were registered under conservation category. Five species are in degree of
threat, corresponding to Gomortega keule, Pitavia punctata and Vestia foetida in the Endangered category,
and, Asplenium trilobum and Citronella mucronata in the Vulnerable category. The remaining 14 taxa correspond
to species not considered threatened (Near Threatened and Least Concern categories).
In relation to frequency, the introduced shrub Teline monspessulana was the most recorded species,
followed by the native tree Aristotelia chilensis and the introduced species Eucalyptus globulus, Rubus
ulmifolius and Pinus radiata. Other representative species of the AI were the endemic Peumus trees
boldus and Cryptocarya alba, the native trees Persea lingue, Luma apiculata and Nothofagus obliqua, the
shrubs Muehlenbeckia hastulata, Cissus striata, Lapageria rosea and Boquila trifoliolata, and, herbs
Bomarea salsilla, Chusquea quila, Blechnum hastatum, Lobelia tupa and Geranium core-core.
Regarding the abundance of species in the threatened category, the species counted were Gomortega
keule, Pitavia punctata and Citronella mucronata, the first two in the Endangered category and the last
considered Vulnerable. In total, 606 specimens were registered, where the largest record is Gomortega
keule with 470 individuals, followed by Citronella mucronata with 90 specimens and finally Pitavia
punctata with 46.

Environmental Singularities: Based on the review and analysis of the data collected, it was determined that
the entire area of native forest preservation in the area of influence of the Project (equivalent to
36.28 ha) is considered a unique plant formation or one of low national representativeness. The condition
of native forest preservation is given by the presence of specimens of Citronella mucronata
(Vulnerable), Gomortega keule (Endangered) and Pitavia Punctata (Endangered). Likewise, 22.99 ha of forest
native preservation are considered a relict, relic, remnant and fragile plant formation5. These
formations correspond to those native forests with the presence of specimens of Gomortega keule
(In Danger) and Pitavia Punctata (In Danger).
Three species regulated by special protection measures were recorded, these correspond to
Gomortega keule and Pitavia punctata, which are declared natural monuments; and the species
Lapageria rosea (copihue) which has a special regulation prohibiting commercialization,
In addition, it is declared as the national flower.
Considering the regional endemic origin of the species, in the area of influence the
presence of species with restricted distribution, nor the presence of species located close to
its geographical distribution limit nor species located close to its altitudinal limit.
The area of ​​influence of the project is not located within SNASPE units. On the other hand, the AI does not
overlaps with, or is not within the boundaries of, some type of priority conservation site
national (of the five that are located in the Biobío Region: Isla Mocha marine area, Cerro Cayumanque,
Nonguén Farm, Nevados de Chillán and Quebrada Caramávida). Of the 47 priority sites for
conservation of the biodiversity of the Biobío Region, the AI of the Project does not overlap either, or does not
is inside
the limits of any priority site for the conservation of diversity defined in the
regional strategies. Likewise, it does not overlap, or is not within the boundaries of any area under
official protection, private protection area or protection area according to Law No. 18,378,
corresponding to those called “soil, forest and water conservation districts”. The AI
does not overlap, or is not within the boundaries of any type of Wetland listed in the Inventory
National Wetlands, of the Ministry of the Environment, whose last update is from 2020.
According to the Climate Risk Atlas (ARCLIM-MMA), there is a susceptibility to change
climate at the ecosystem level due to loss of flora, greenness or vigor of the native forest and fires
forestry in the commune of Penco, where the area of influence of the Project is located. It is estimated
Furthermore, for the same commune a very high value for the risk index of biodiversity loss
of flora due to changes in precipitation and temperature, as a result of the future change in the conditions of
average annual precipitation and average annual temperature. At the species level, the most
5 Relict: remnant of an ancient biota that populated the territory in the past, under climatic conditions different from the current ones.
Relic: vegetation that represents what were original vegetation formations, many of which have already disappeared, of great
naturalness, constituting an example and invaluable source of information, and which today are greatly reduced.
Remnant: it is understood as what remains of what was in its natural state.
Fragile: weak, which can deteriorate easily

b) Fungi and Lichens
Fungi (macromycetes): 92 taxa of fungi were recorded in the area of influence of the Project, 61
were identified at the specific level, while the remaining 31 were identified at the level of
gender. Systematically, the taxa are organized within the kingdom Fungi, into 2 Phyla, 8 Classes, 17
Orders, 53 families and 81 genera. Of the latter, the genera Crepidotus and Entoloma were the best
represented, with 3 taxa each. In relation to families, the best represented corresponded to
Strophariaceae, with 7 taxa, followed by the families Agaricaceae, Inocybaceae, Pyronemataceae and
Xylariaceae with 4 taxa each.
The environmental distribution of macromycete records in the AI, according to land use
indicated in the flora and vegetation component, indicate that the greatest number of species were observed
in forest plantation type land use (54 taxa), followed by native forest (23 taxa) and forest
feral (22). The rest of the land uses present richness under the 15 species, being grassland the
which presents the smallest number of registered taxa (8).
The species that showed the highest frequency in the AI corresponded to Stereum hirsutum, present in a
58.1% of the surveyed sampling points (absolute frequency). On the other hand, this species
It represented 7.1% of the fungal records made in this study (relative frequency). It follows
Fraternal laccaria with 51.6% absolute frequency and 6.3% representativeness with respect to the registry
total species. S. hirsutum is a fungus that develops on dead wood, it is common to find it
growing on fallen branches in the forest, or on stumps in meadows. It also grows in
wood used for fences and for constructions. L. fraterna is found along trails and clearings
herbaceous eucalyptus plantations (even burned by fire), other times, under acacias or
oaks (Campi et al. 2017).
Three species were registered in the conservation category, Anthracophyllum discolor, Cortinarius
magellanicus and Descolea antarctica, all listed as Least Concern. About these species
the following can be noted: A. discolor corresponds to a species d
and agaricoid fungus, degrading
wood, abundant and widely distributed in forest environments; C. magellanicus and D. antarctica
correspond to ectomycorrhizal fungi, associated with Nothofagus species.
Lichens: 52 taxa were recorded in the area of influence of the Project, 38 were identified at the
specific, while 14 taxa were identified at the genus level. Systematically, the taxa
They are organized within the kingdom Fungi, in 1 Phylum, 3 Classes, 12 Orders, 22 families and 33 genera. Regarding
To the genera, Chrysothrix, Cladonia, Lecanora, Leptogium and Parmotrema were the best represented,
with 3 species each. The family with the best representation was Parmeliaceae with 10 taxa, followed
of Lecanoraceae with 4 taxa.

The environmental distribution of lichen records in the AI indicates that the greatest number of species,
Like the records of macromycete fungi, it occurs in forest plantation-type land use (32

The rest of the environments present richness under the nine species, again being grassland the one that

with open canopies (Kashiwadani, 1990). In relation to relative frequency, each species represented a

as Data Deficient (Pseudocyphellaria hirsuta).
c) Invertebrate fauna
The area of influence has geomorphological and vegetation characteristics that facilitate the attraction and
protection of arthropods. Among these characteristics are the orographic undulations that give
place to ravines in the relief, where the accumulation of native flora and the presence of water courses
They increase the attraction of these organisms. In turn, mainly plant species are observed
such as Eucalyptus globulus and Pinus radiata, which protect refuges for the prospective species
on the surface of the trunks, in the galleries that can occur in old trunks and in the leaf litter
generated in the soil, while the natural-native vegetation, which is found to a lesser extent
distributed in the area of influence, whether forest associations or bushes, also serves as habitat
for arthropods. In the AI, it was possible to determine, through two field campaigns, an accumulated
247 species.
During the summer campaign, 109 species were recorded, highlighting the Insecta classes (95%),
Entognatha (2%) and Arachnida (3%). In terms of abundance, the largest participation is from the Insecta class
with 1,686 individuals, concentrated in the Coleoptera order (68.8% of the total number of specimens), in which
The most abundant species in the campaign stand out, which are Ceroglossus chilensis cyanicollis
with 218 individuals (all predators), species of the family Leiodidae with 203 individuals (all
polyphagous) and species of the Trogossitidae family with 144 individuals (all detritivores). It also fits
highlight the order Diptera (11.5%), in which species of the Phoridae family stand out with an abundance
of 35 individuals (all fungivores), the genus Apiochaeta with an abundance of 18 individuals (a
saprophagous and the other fungivores) and the Tipulidae family with 17 specimens, all of which are pollinators.

Regarding the ecological attributes of the total number of species found in the summer campaign, these are
They stood out for being mostly predators with 31% of the total number of registered individuals, 28%

During the winter campaign, a total of 160 species were recorded. In these, the Insecta classes stood out
(71%), Entognatha (11%) and Arachnida (13%). In this case the abundance of insects is also found
concentrated in the Coleoptera order (34.4%), in which the family with the greatest abundance stands out in the

which increased compared to the summer campaign, in which families stand out for their abundance
Tipulidae with 115 individuals (all pollinators) and Phoridae with 42 individuals (all fungivores).

species stood out for being mostly detritivores with 33% of the total number of individuals recorded,
27% predators and 12% fungivores, thus representing saprophages, phytophages, hematophages,
soil engineers, omnivores, parasitoids, pollinators, root-eaters and xylophages in smaller percentages
from 0.1% to 13.1%.
In summary, in both campaigns predators stood out for their abundance, and in terms of
number of species recorded in each one, it was determined that the diversity of the community is on average
to high with a highest value of 3.11 in the PME15 during summer and 3.37 in the PME5 during winter.
Of the 247 species registered, four have conservation considerations and one of them is in the category
of threat. Ceroglossus chilensis cyanicollis (Least Concern), Euathlus manicata (Near Threatened),
Acanthogonathus pissi (Least Concern) and Phrixotrichus scrofa (Endangered). As for the
abundance of these four species, in total 224 specimens were recorded where the largest record is
Ceroglossus chilensis cyanicollis with 220 individuals, followed by Phrixotrichus scrofa with two individuals and
finally Euathlus manicata and Acanthogonathus pissi, both with a single specimen, the latter being
species endemic to Chile.
d) Vertebrate Fauna
73 species of native terrestrial fauna were recorded (48 records in autumn, 48 in winter, 49 in
spring, 57 in summer and 47 in a second spring campaign) and correspond to two amphibians, seven
reptiles, 49 birds and 15 mammals. Of all the species identified, 38.4% (28 species) are
classified within some conservation category according to current national legislation. These include
two amphibians, seven reptiles, seven birds and 12 mammals. Three of these species are classified as
threatened, specifically in the Vulnerable category: an amphibian, Eupsophus roseus (Rana rosacea
of leaf litter); a reptile, Liolaemus schroederi (Schröeder's lizard), and, a mammal, Pudu puda (Pudú).

is (54 species, which

Under the conditions of average annual precipitation and average annual temperature, an index of

e) Continental aquatic ecosystems
Benthic macroinvertebrates
Phytobenthos: Similar records were observed during the winter and summer campaigns. In the first one
recorded the presence of 16 taxa of phytobenthic cells, seven identified at a specific level,
while the remaining nine were identified at the genus level. In the second campaign
recorded 18 taxa, 10 identified at a specific level, while the remaining eight at the level of
gender. In both, the Bacillariophyceae group stood out for its richness with seven genera, highlighting
the species Cymbella cistula for being present in all sampling stations, however,
particularly in summer, the record of Asterionella formosa was also relevant due to its greater
abundance. For the Fragilariophyceae group, three genera were recorded in both campaigns,
The species Diatoma vulgaris stands out for its high abundance, which reached an average of 1,217
Cel/mm2 in P9 in winter and an average of 1,064 Cel/mm2 in P7 and P9 in summer. On the other hand, the
Coscinodiscophyceae group was represented by one genus in winter and by two in summer, while
that Cyanophyceae and Zygnematophyceae counted c
with a gender in both campaigns.
Regarding benthic biomass, described as the concentration of chlorophyll a and which is useful for
determine the primary productivity of a given ecosystem (Samanez et al., 2014), this reached
the highest value in the P23 season in both campaigns with 0.848 mg g-1 for winter and 0.973 mg g-1
for summer.
On the other hand, the spring campaign recorded the presence of 26 taxa, four identified at the
specific, while the remaining 22 were identified at the genus level. Standing out for its wealth

These larvae play a fundamental role in the processing of different types of organic matter.
(coarse and fine), due to the morpho-behavioral variety for the acquisition of food, in addition to
be a link between producers (phytoplankton and benthic algae) and secondary consumers. They are
considered opportunistic omnivores, which eat a wide variety of foods and, in general,
report a low degree of selectivity in their diet, being classified as generalists. These consume
different types of food such as algae, macrophytes, wood remains, macroinvertebrates, detritus and
microorganisms. By analyzing stomach contents, the Chironomidae can be designated
different trophic guilds: carnivores, detritivores, algivores, periphytivores and omnivores (Cummins & Klug,

collecting detritus from the surface of rocks and wood, belonging to the functional trophic group of
gatherers, while organisms of the family Baetidae can be gatherers or browsers
(Palma, 2013).

3.4.5.3 Types of ecosystems based
to vegetation floors
Based on the vegetation floor classification proposal of Luebert and Pliscoff (2019), the Project is
located in two formations. One corresponds to the Sclerophyllous Forest, specifically the vegetation floor
Mediterranean coastal sclerophyllous forest of Lithrea caustica - Azara integrifolia, and the other called
formation of the Deciduous Forest, specifically the vegetation floor Mediterranean deciduous forest
coastal Nothofagus obliqua - Gomortega keule (Figure 3-134).

3.4.5.4 Ecosystem relationships
i. Terrestrial Ecosystem
The concept of ecosystem is essential in ecology, although its understanding goes beyond a simple
definition. Tansley (1935) defined the ecosystem as a biotic community and its associated physical environment.
in a specific place. In the context of this Project, the biotic components establish various
functional groups that interact with each other and with abiotic elements. These interactions focus
mainly in the flows of matter and energy present in areas with vegetation and water courses,
where different elements of the ecosystem are intertwined and various trophic chains are established.
In the relationships between soil and plants, it is crucial to recognize that soil is not only vital for
growth of plants, but the latter also play a fundamental role in the
formation and functionality of the soil, because plant roots are a crucial factor in the
prevention of soil erosion. Furthermore, when plants die, their decomposition provides
the raw material necessary for worms, insects and microorganisms to generate elements rich in
nutrients, essential for maintaining strong food webs and promoting healthy soil structure
healthy. The removal of the vegetation layer can generate several impacts on the soil, such as
variations in water availability due to increased evapotranspiration, variations in
temperature and aeration, loss of nutrients and organic matter, erosion and definitive loss of the
soil structure.
In relation to the flows and interrelationships between ecosystems, it can be inferred that the effects of cutting
of vegetation can extend beyond the area where it is executed, and depend fundamentally
of the capacity of the vegetation to react to local or widespread disturbances depending on the source
that generates them, which generally translates into habitat fragmentation, a process that consists of the
division of an area of continuous habitat into discontinuous remnant fragments that progressively become
become smaller, and separated from each other, by a matrix where environmental conditions may not
be favorable for the species of the original habitat (Fahrig, 2003). This aspect is notable in the area of the
Project, where forest plantations abound. Forest plantations were inserted decades ago
on the slopes of the hills and the remaining vegetation has also undergone changes, where the strong
3-328
==================================================================================
PAGE 344
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
presence of introduced plant species, often being the dominant species (species such as
Acacia dealbata, Acacia melanoxylon, Eucalyptus globulus and Pinus radiata).
Among the ecosystem processes that occur within the area of influence, those that stand out
developed by lower trophic levels, directly related to nutrient cycling, as well as
as the different elements of the ecosystem that intervene in them. The organizations in charge of
decomposition are particularly relevant in this case, and show pulse dynamics, with
peaks of activities during periods of greater water availability. Thus, the bacterial activity and
fungal disease is limited temporally and spatially to those areas where humidity is conserved
for longer (ravines or watercourses) or where radiation is temporary
l looks reduced (below
of bushes and trees). On the other hand, in open areas most of the scarce organic activity
existing is attributable to the action of saprophytic (decomposer) species, among which the
mushrooms.
From an ecological point of view, many fungal species are free-living saprobes, which is very
relevant, because they are generally the primary decomposer organisms in their environments.
This characteristic allows them to use a wide variety of materials as substrates, which can affect
negatively to human beings by destroying their food and many elements used in their activities. Others
Fungi are biotrophic or parasitic, negatively affecting both animals and plants. Others are
mutualists, establishing symbiotic relationships with plants, animals and prokaryotes. Lichens or Fungi
Lichenized and mycorrhizae (root-bound fungi) are two successful examples of the latter.
(Alexopoulos et al., 1996). Lichens are highly successful mutualistic ectosymbionts among members of the
Kingdom Fungi and different groups of algae, including cyanobacteria, although recent work indicates the
more or less stable presence of a third fungal component of a constitutive nature (Spribille et al.,
2016). Lichenization, in turn, can be considered as the form occupied by fungi, which are
heterotrophic organisms, for the acquisition of new properties and forms of nutrition (Barreno,
1998). Lichens are capable of colonizing a wide range of substrates, not only natural surfaces.
provided by rocks, soil, bark, decorticated wood, leaves and animal shells, but also
industrial materials (Brightman & Seaward, 1977). These characteristics have allowed them to be part
constitutive of almost all environments where life is possible (Sancho et al., 2006).
Fauna is an important factor in the dispersion of propagules of mycorrhizal fungi.
in forest ecosystems. Different ways exist to disperse mycorrhizal propagules both
horizontally as well as vertically, for example, through ingestion or adhesion to external structures
of animals. In the case of invertebrates, there is the potential for the spread of mycorrhizal fungi.
in the different stages of development, as well as by their mobility in activities carried out for their
feeding and burrow construction. The effective dispersion of mycorrhizal fungi is carried out
especially through springtails, mites, earthworms, nematodes, ants, isopods,
diplopods, crustaceans, diptera, wasps, beetles and grasshoppers. Fungal dispersal can also
be favored by different groups of vertebrates, such as mammals, birds, reptiles and amphibians.

Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Standing out mainly are rodents and birds, who disperse mycorrhizal fungi over long periods of time.
distances due to the consumption of propagules attached to their food and external structures (Naranjo-Morán
et al., 2021).
All biological communities are intrinsically related and plants are no exception; of
In fact, they interact with each other in different ways, both negatively and positively. The interaction
negative can occur, for example, due to competition to obtain essential resources such as electricity, water,
nutrients, space and also for the services of pollinators and seed dispersers. Another type of
Negative interaction occurs when plants use resources that other plants have acquired,
parasitizing them. On the other hand, when the relationships are positive, different plant species benefit
in various ways by the presence of others, for example, minimizing the impact of a hostile climate,
facilitating access or, directly, providing vital resources. There are many types of interactions
positive effects between neighboring plants that include attraction of pollinators, nitrogen fixation (a
essential resource) from the atmosphere or the transfer of nutrients through mycorrhizae. The
Interactions between plants can be very specific, when the relationship occurs only
between two species,
or general when there are several species of plants that interact. The interactions can be very
varied; plants that grow on other plants, plants that help others grow, plants
that compete with each other for light, water or space.
In an integrated analysis of the biotic components of the area of influence, they are considered
fundamentally four vegetation systems: the native forest formation, the scrub formation, the
grassland-prairie and plantations, referring to the most relevant constituent elements. The
Native forests have been replaced by exotic plantations, generating a mosaic of fragments
of natural forests immersed in a matrix of plantations, creating a complex ecosystem in which
Organisms actively move across the landscape and connect habitats in space and time, being
essential components in the dynamics of ecosystem development and its resilience (Lundberg &
Moberg 2003).
The diversity of primary producers in the terrestrial ecosystem is determined by cloudiness and
humidity that allows the development of open and semi-dense scrub structures dominated by Fuchsia
magellanica, Rubus ulmifolius and Teline monspessulana. Likewise, the native tree formation that is
presents fragmented interior pine and eucalyptus plantations that dominate the landscape of the AI, this
characterized by the dominant species Aetoxicon punctatum, Aristotella chilensis, Laurerlia
sempervirens, Notofagus obliqua and Peumus boldus.
The consumption of plants by animals, herbivores, is the main channel of redistribution of solar energy
between living beings at different trophic levels. Within forests, herbivores generate
very sparse undergrowth and especially impoverished in palatable plants such as maqui (Aristotelia
chilensis), the grill (Ribes magellanicum) or the pañil (Buddleja globosa).

The least visible and difficult to recognize actors in community dynamics are insects and
micromammals that, silent, protected at night and hidden among the leaves, but not by
Those less effective will consume most of the seedlings before they reach their first year of growth.
life. Most of the seeds produced will not germinate either, they will be consumed
by insects on the same plant and rodents once they have reached the ground. Although insects
herbivores are very abundant but small in size, the damage they produce individually affects
only a small part of the plant, for example a leaf or part of a stem. On the other hand, mammals
herbivores, which are larger, are less abundant but the damage they produce individually
It can affect a large part of the plant, such as entire branches, or even the entire plant. Are these
diverse forms of interaction between individuals and between species that give dynamism to communities
vegetables-animals. This interaction allows the circulation of energy and matter, modeling the structure
and landscape dynamics.
The forest is home to a rich and varied fauna of invertebrates involved in pollination. Among these, the
The most common pollinators are insects such as bees, bumblebees and some wasps (all belonging to
to the Hymenoptera group), as well as a wide variety of relatives of flies and mosquitoes
(Diptera) (Chalcoff et al., 2014). The diversity and composition of plants and pollinators present, together
with the analysis of which species of pollinators visit which plants and how often, are factors
determinants that define the structure of a network of mutualistic interactions. The interaction between
Plants and pollinators follow a fairly predictable pattern in terms of time and location. For what
Therefore, it is not surprising that many insect predators take advantage of this association and
use flowers as preferred hunting sites. This strategy is used by spiders, mantises, dragonflies
and some species of wasps. Most of these predators use their camouflage ability
to stalk their prey inside or
around the flowers. Furthermore, the presence of lizards reduces
significantly the frequency and duration of visits by pollinating insects.
Continuing with terrestrial invertebrates, predators function as controllers of
population density of other species (Sánchez et al. 1997), as does Ceroglossus chilensis
cyanicollis. In this species, both adults and their larval stages are mainly predators.
(carnivores) and feed on worms and insect larvae in general (Jiroux, 2006). Joseph (1931) and
Ruiz (1936) point out that adults of C. chilensis prey on insects (larvae), earthworms and carrion.
of small vertebrates.
On the other hand, detritivorous and saprophagous organisms are part of the group of invertebrates that
They intervene in the decomposition processes of organic remains of animal or plant origin,
contributing greatly to the redistribution of remains and formation of the topsoil layer.
Thanks to the action of these arthropods during the initial phase of fragmentation, the organic remains
They can be degraded. These remains, if they did not decompose, could
accumulate in the soil in the form of unused organic matter. In this sense it can be stated that
in a terrestrial ecosystem in which there is no entomofauna capable of acting effectively on the
