plant and animal remains, serious alterations will end up occurring that will inevitably lead
in an alteration of it and consequently the loss of biodiversity (Galante & Marcos, 1997).
Many plant species, some endemic, depend for their reproduction and continuity
population of a single species of pollinator, the lesser hummingbird (Sephanoides sephaniodes), a phenomenon
called ornithophily (Aizen et al. 2002). In the specific case of the Project AI, the plant species that
were described in the baseline and that are pollinated by this bird correspond to: Lapageria rosea
(copihue, shrub-like habit and endemic biogeographic origin), Fuchsia magellanica (chilco, shrub,
native), Tristerix corymbosus (quintral, hemiparasitic shrub, native) and Rhaphithamnus spinosus (myrtle
male, bush, native) (Smith-Ramírez, 1993; Aizen et al. 2002). The relationships between plants and
Pollinators are very important for the survival of the species involved and the dynamics of the
ecosystems, so its alteration can compromise the life of pollinators, reproduction
sexuality of plants and also affect other associated organisms in this network of interactions.
The diversity of birds and mammals in the temperate forests of southern South America is low compared to
with other forests of the subcontinent. At least 56 species of birds occur frequently in the
forests or will establish nests in these habitats, including aquatic or grassland species closely
associated with the forest for their reproduction (Jaksic & Feinsinger, 1991, Altamirano et al. 2012). an attribute
of these assemblages of mammals and birds is that their composition changes through the different types of
forest. Organisms that actively move across the landscape and connect habitats in space and space.
time (wide home ranges) are essential components in the dynamics of the development of the
ecosystem and its resilience (Lundberg and Moberg, 2003).
Species richness is often associated with habitats modified by small mammals (Davidson
et al. 2012). Birds and small mammals often play different ecological roles. one
association between birds and small mammals in habitats modified by the activity of
micromammals could result in a coupling between different ecosystem processes at the scale of
landscape. Micromammal assemblages are numerically dominated by two or three species,
usually Abrothrix longipilis accompanied by Abrothrix olivaceus and Oligoryzomys longicaudatus.
In functional terms, birds and large mammals play an important role as consumers.
primary, secondary and tertiary, intervening in the flows of
e matter and energy of the ecosystem. The
herbivorous species directly affect vegetation structure and nutrient cycles through
the consumption of leaves and stems (Ostfeld & Keesing, 2000; Sekercioglu et al., 2004). Other ways, through
consumption of fruits, seeds and nectar, intervene in the dispersion and pollination of plants, affecting
also the structure of the vegetation and the processes of regeneration and ecological succession, as well as the
transmission of parasitic plants such as quintral (Armesto et al. 1987). For their part, the species
carnivores (including insectivores) have an indirect effect on the vegetation by preying on the
herbivores, affecting their abundance, diversity and behavior (Ostfeld & Keesing, 2000; Sekercioglu
et al., 2004; Ray et al., 2005; Casula et al., 2006; Farias 2012). On the other hand, Muñoz-Pedreros et al. (2010)

relate the relative abundance of small mammals to three types of environments: Acacia savanna
caven (hawthorn surrounded by grassland), sclerophyllous forest (Peumus boldus, Quillaja saponaria, Cryptocaria
alba, Schinus latifolius), mixed scrub (Acccia dealbata, Maytenus boaria, Quillaja saponaria, Schinus
latifolius) and the herbaceous stratum represented by L. saxatilis, A. capillaris, Avena barbata, Festuca sp., P.
annua, Briza minor and Bromus hordeaceus, all of them environments similar to those of the area of influence of the
project.
The understanding of trophic structure is limited, which can bias the estimation of the diet of the
predator due to several factors, such as variability in sampling or temporal fluctuations
and spatial in the composition of the diet. This complexity is intensified when communities
They include both migratory and resident populations. It is crucial to understand the occupied habitats, the
hunting strategies, the abundance and temporal variability of prey, the size relationship between prey
and predator, the periods of prey activity, and the breadth and overlap of trophic niche between
coexisting species. Consumer response to changes in resources is extremely
varied between different species, which adds an additional layer of complexity.
Relationships between predators and prey are understood to be two-way interactions. The
Predators exert direct effects on their prey by injuring or killing them, and indirect effects on the
population structure and the organization of assemblages, such as reducing the number of competitors
potentials. Likewise, prey can directly influence their predators by determining
their presence and abundance, and indirectly by providing an axis of resources along which
Predators can either group together forming multispecific guilds or segregate by performing a
resource partitioning, where prey level indirectly affects diversity and relative abundance
of species at the trophic level of the predator (Jaksic & Simonetti, 1987).
It is also important to consider the implications beyond the direct relationship between predators and
their prey, taking into account the resource sources of the prey, especially when it comes to the
ecosystem conservation. Through their feeding activity, birds, for example, can
play additional roles as pollinators, seed dispersal agents, and regulators of
prey populations (Root-Berstein & Jaksic, 2013). Therefore, some species of birds, lizards and
Foxes that consume fruits contribute significantly to seed dispersal and the maintenance of
the vegetation formations of the AI.
Granivorous rodents, such as the olive mouse (A. olivaceus), the woolly mouse (A. longipilis), and the
longtail (O. longicaudatus), may act as occasional seed dispersers in forests
tempered. These three species primarily consume fruits of Aetoxicon punctatus and Gevuina.
hazelnut, and smaller amounts of other berries (Housse, 1953; Miller & Rottmann, 1976; Yañez & Jaksic,
1978; Armesto et al., 1987). This is also observed in the case
or birds such as the thrush, the tench and the
Gay's cometocino, which, like rodents, are prey to raptors and therefore part of the network
trophic. Rodents and birds consume fruits and berries of native plants such as Litre (Lithrea caustica),

Maitén (Maytenus boaria) and the Boldo (Peumus boldus) (Armesto et al., 1987; Marín, 2012). The birds
granivores, among which are Curaeus curaeus, Diuca diuca and Zonotrichia capensis, tend to
show different feeding site preferences and different feeding behaviors
with small mammals (Sekercioglu, 2006), so they can share sites with similar resources

disperse plant species, it is the only quintral disperser in forest environments, which in turn is

Birds and small mammals can also be complementary in the sense that birds act
frequently as seed dispersers, and the modifications of small mammals in the soil
can create patches of bare or protected soil that enhance the establishment and recruitment of
seedlings (Kremen, 2005).
In the area of influence of the project, the top vertebrate predator assemblage is found
made up mainly of birds of prey and a smaller number of carnivorous mammals, although
We can also consider the long-tailed snake (Philodrias chamissonis) due to its presence
potential. For most species in the raptor community, the existence of bird habitat
nesting provided by the woody layer is a limiting factor, affecting the latter, in a way
indirectly, the levels of carnivory in the AI. On the other hand, there is a very rich assemblage of insectivorous birds
which would account for a large part of the consumption of invertebrates, to which are added mammals such as
bats Tadarida brasiliensis, Myotis chiloensis, Lasiurus varius, Histiotus macrotus, Histiotus
magellanicus, Lasiurus cinereus and the common woolly mouse (Abrothrix longipilis), making a contribution
substantial to the total consumption of invertebrates.
When analyzing the probable predator-prey relationships, a high trophic overlap is observed in terms of
refers to mammals and birds of prey, although they also share other types of prey in their diet (reptiles,
amphibians and insects) with variable importance. The coexistence of predators would be supported by
the way available resources are divided. They could potentially be competitors or just
share the resource when predation is differential, for example, generated by differences in the
diurnal or nocturnal activity of the predator and prey, or differences in prey size. When
several predators establish themselves in the same area, interact directly or indirectly and coincide
in space and time, they can use, share or compete for common resources (Jaksic, 2001).
They can even determine many of the characteristics of a community due to changes
exerted on the population and behavior of their prey and/or competitive segregation with other predators.
One of the key predators is the puma (Puma concolor), which plays a crucial role as a predator.
top, regulating the populations of its prey and maintaining a balance in the ecosystem. Your presence
It is essential to control herbivore populations, avoiding overexploitation of resources
plants, an essential component in the trophic chain, influencing the structure and function of
ecosystems where it lives. Its presence guarantees the conservation of biodiversity and the maintenance

ii. Continental Aquatic Ecosystem
The continental aquatic ecosystem of the area of influence is presented as an ecosystem of streams and
bodies of water with different runoff, both shallow and deep, with gentle slopes,
sectors covered with terrestrial vegetation, with balls of different sizes and sandy sediments
thick, which generates a great variety of habitats so that as the habitat varies, so does
the food web and the structure of the ecosystem are modified. The concept of continuity of the river (Vannote
et al., 1980, Minshall et al., 1983) has been instrumental in suggesting how functional trophic relationships
change from headwaters to downstream with associated gradients of riparian vegetation and
geomorphology of the channel, being at the same time important the fragmentation and heterogeneity of the habitat (Poff
& Ward, 1990, Fausch et al., 2002).
All energy in the food web begins with primary production or photosynthesis, but not
necessarily in the current itself. Much of the food consumed grows directly
in the stream, such as algae, diatoms, nymphs, larvae and fish. In the lower section of the stream, the production
Energy comes mainly from photosynthesizing algae and aquatic plants. The upper reaches
and tributaries may not support algae and aquatic plants due to lack of sunlight given the cover
of the forest canopy. The organisms in these parts of the river depend on external or allochthonous contributions to
obtain usable energy. Allochthonous energy inputs originate from outside the system and include
leaves, trunks, living and dead animal matter and dissolved organic carbon.
In the temperate deciduous forest, as the daylight shortens, the leaves will begin to fall and many will be
carried away or fall directly into the headwaters of the streams and will flow to and through the entire
system, grouping in points of imprisonment. These sheet packs provide a source
of food for bacteria and aquatic insects that live in the stream. Microorganisms help
change the chemistry of the leaf litter and make it more assimilable and nutritious for the next group that colonizes
bundles of leaves: invertebrates and crustaceans, breaking them down and finally converting them
in fine particles of organic matter. At the same time, nutrients are leached directly into the water.
of the stream from decomposing leaves as dissolved organic matter. aquatic invertebrates
that eat leaf packets include some species of the orders Plecoptera, Tricoptera, Diptera,
Coleoptera and snails (Mollusca). These chew the entire leaf or scrape the surface of the leaf like
feeding method. In this way, aquatic insects can significantly accelerate the
decomposition of leaves in streams.
In this part of the stream food web, nutrients are released directly through leaching
from leaves to stream water, and also as decomposed organic carbon circulating through
the food network. Bacteria and fine organic matter are devoured by grinders,
crustaceans and small fish, and then other invertebrates eat the smaller invertebrates
and fish eat larger invertebrates. Therefore, the fallen leaves of the headboards
Forests form the food and nutrient base of the entire stream ecosystem.

The aquatic vertebrates recorded were two fish species: Trichomycterus areolatus (catfish) and
Oncorhyncus mykiss (rainbow trout). The catfish, with benthic habits, lives in soft substrates at
hard in waters with almost no runoff to fast-flowing streams. Their carnivorous diet includes a number
of different taxa, mainly aquatic insects such as Chironomidae, Trichoptera,
Ephemeroptera, Oligochaeta, Baetidae, Elmidae, Plecoptera and Hyalella (Chará et al. 2006) and more prey
large, such as decapods and dragonfly larvae (nymphs). Rainbow trout is a high trophic level fish.
which feeds on zooplankton in its fry, followed by insects (Ephemeroptera, Trichoptera and
Diptera), crustaceans and smaller fish as they grow (Hardy, 2002). Unlike catfish,
Rainbow trout use the water column and the substrate as habitat. Several species of waterfowl
They feed on these fish, and from another point of view, these birds play a crucial ecological role
by carrying fish eggs, plankton, algae and other aquatic microorganisms on their legs or feathers
between different bodies of water, facilitating a vital exchange between distant sectors, both in
small as in large bodies of water (Silva et al., 2019). On the other hand, there are also other species
of aquatic birds, such as the pintail (Laterallus jamaicensis), present in the Project AI, which is
It feeds mainly on aquatic and terrestrial invertebrates (Martínez & González, 2017).
Nutrients promote the growth of microorganisms that can break down matter
organic; Often, nutrient-promoted excess algae coincides with and feeds this, and the
Microbial decomposition dramatically reduces environmental dissolved oxygen levels and changes the
pH, which in turn has adverse effects on invertebrates and fish found in the stream
(Smith et al., 2003; Hilton et al., 2006; Ferreira et al., 2015; Dodds & Smith, 2016). primary production
of benthic algae from streams
responds to elevated CO levels, resulting in greater
2
quantities of low-quality algae for food for invertebrate consumers (Hargrave et al.
2009). Microorganisms have the ability to extract nutrients from the environment and decompose detritus.
that initially lack nutrients, which improves the nutritional quality of these elements. This to your
increases the attraction for detritus-feeding invertebrates and helps overcome the
growth limitations caused by nutrient shortages (Evans‐White & Halvorson, 2017;
Ferreira et al., 2015; Guo et al., 2016). Nutrient fortification generally changes the
riparian invertebrate communities to be dominated by Ephemeroptera, Plecoptera and Tricoptera
to those dominated by worms, snails and mosquitoes (Ballantine & Davies-Colley, 2014; Tonkin et al.,
2013). Surviving fish species may have poor conditions as a result of stress and
changes in the diet of macroinvertebrate communities altered or of low nutritional quality
(Baker et al., 2013; Schreck & Tort, 2016; Sealey & Gatlin, 1999; Thera et al., 2020).
Riparian plant species affect nutrient cycling directly through absorption, use and
nutrient loss and indirectly influencing microbial activity and herbivory (Hobbie,
1992). Although the composition of riparian communities changes with nutrient enrichment
(Camargo & Alonso, 2006; Smith and Schindler, 2009), the influence of these changes on the properties and
Emerging functioning of the food web is not well understood (Boersma et al., 2008; Friberg et
al., 2011).

3.4.5.5 Forcing factors
For the ecosystems described in the AI, the forcing factors of anthropic origin are related to the
occupation of the territory due to changes in land use for industrial and urban activity, in
different degrees of land occupation, which reduces and fragments the environments where they develop.
There are also forcing factors of natural origin, which restrict the development of ecosystems.
and their ecological properties that maintain them. These correspond mainly to availability of
water (both surface and underground aquifers), the physical-chemical quality of the water, eutrophication,
soil erosion and quality (main substrate), non-native species, forest fires and climate change
(the latter as a mix between anthropic and natural).
Regarding the aquatic ecosystem, although it is recognized that nutrient enrichment promotes a
excessive growth of algae and other microorganisms, this phenomenon can alter both directly and
indirectly the composition of the ecological community. These changes may modify the importance
trophic of species, which in turn affects the ability of communities to adapt (resilience)
to high levels of nutrient enrichment or flooding events. The enrichment of
nutrients can result in communities composed of energy-inefficient species
with high communal respiration (excluding microorganisms
nisms). If community respiration were several
times higher in enriched communities, this can lead to hypoxic conditions even without
concomitant changes in microbial respiration. Enriched communities exhibit cascades
weaker trophic organisms, which can generate less robustness in the face of loss of energy flow. The
enriched communities will be more affected structurally and functionally by species sensitive to
altering the flow, which would make these communities more vulnerable.
Dissolved inorganic nitrogen (DIN) and dissolved reactive phosphorus (DRP) are the dominant culprits of
eutrophication, both limit the growth of both autotrophs (generally algae and macrophytes) and
of heterotrophs (generally bacteria and invertebrates), base organisms of trophic chains
(Camargo & Alonso, 2006, Smith & Schindler, 2009).
Climate change can significantly alter stream temperatures and water regimes.
flow, as well as the adjacent terrestrial ecosystems through which water, nutrients and
organic matter reach water bodies (Palmer et al., 2009). The changes in said contributions to the
Streams may also manifest through climate-mediated changes in water regimes.
terrestrial disturbance, such as wildfires and associated debris flows (Davis et al., 2013),
with the consequent positive or negative consequences for fish populations (Harris et al., 2015;
Spencer et al., 2003) and their predators. Fish responses to higher temperatures
Warm weather driven by climate change depends on whether increasing metabolic demands can
be satisfied through the productivity of lower trophic levels (Brett et al. 1969; Beakes et al., 2014).
Climate change also directly affects phenology and community resource structure

==================================================================================
PAGE 358
==================================================================================

Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
of prey, competitors and predators in terrestrial and aquatic ecosystems (Burgmer et al., 2007).
Interactions between species can strongly influence how climate change affects animals.
organisms at all scales by altering their individual fitness, geographic distribution, and structure and
dynamics of your community. Some species can produce changes in the flow of nutrients so
refers to availability, inputs, recycling and loss in the ecosystem.
One of the key ways climate change or other stressors affect ecosystems
river flows is causing changes in the flow of rivers that reflect the interaction of precipitation,
temperature, soils, geology and land cover. The variation with respect to your regime of
Natural flow is critical to the ecological integrity and health of streams and rivers (Poff et al., 1997).
The introduction of functional groups with a higher relative growth rate and size than the species
natives, can increase the primary productivity of an ecosystem and as a consequence reduce water
runoff and river flow (effect of invasive species). The invasion of tree species in
Mediterranean shrub communities such as the introduction and subsequent invasion of Pinus radiata and
Acacia spp. entails an increase in evapotranspiration6 of ecosystems (Vilá, 1998).
Dispersal differences between species could dissolve interactions between existing species,
removing some modules and communities and creating new ones. This "remix" of interactions
can occur rapidly because the abiotic thresholds that limit distribution margins are
move through space, leading to large-scale changes in species dynamics. The
Broken and new interactions will likely alter the abundances, distributions, and probabilities of
extinction of species. The consequences for the focal species depend on: (i) the direct effects
of climate change; (ii) direct effects on interacting species; (iii) degree of specialization
of the species; (iv) strength and climate sensitivity of
the interactions; (v) impacts on mobility; and (vi)
potential for new community composition (Gilman et al. 2010).
3.4.6 Synthesis and Conclusions
The Project, in general terms, is geographically located in the Biobío Region, in the communes of
Penco and Concepción, within a Mediterranean climate zone whose vegetation contains forest and
fragmented sclerophyllous thicket inserted in a pine and eucalyptus plantation area, with presence
of water courses in streams mainly.
The identification of ecosystem relationships is defined as a highly complex work due to
the large number of processes and elements that exist in it. In this Chapter it was possible to identify
the key elements that act as global regulators of terrestrial and aquatic ecosystems
that make up the Project location area (the present ecosystems have been described and
6 Associated with two processes: i) evaporation from the soil and from the surface covered by plants, and ii) transpiration from the
plant leaves.

developed conceptual models for each of them), which allowed reducing the dimension of the
problem, thus obtaining a better identification of environmental singularities and their relationships. A
From the information analyzed for each group of ecosystems it was possible to identify elements that
They act as global regulators.
Through conceptual models, the presence of functional groups can be deduced among the
biotic and abiotic components of the two ecosystems, as well as the driving factors that establish
different relationships, especially those that involve mobility between terrestrial systems and
associated with the availability of water resources. The interactions between plant ecosystems,
considered as the main shapers of the environment due to their particular characteristics, they are
are significantly influenced by their ability to adapt and interact with other elements
present (biotics and abiotics), mainly due to limiting factors in the environment, such as
water availability, soil productivity and wide temperature ranges.
In the case of terrestrial ecosystems, external elements of origin such as precipitation stand out.
and fog that affect many key processes and support the wide diversity observed in the area.
On the other hand, woody plants, birds and mammals are related to many processes of the terrestrial system,
but with the difference that its state depends on internal and external factors to the system, due to
mainly to its wide home range. For the aquatic ecosystem, it is possible to observe that the
Ichthyofauna, algae and invertebrates stand out as those that participate transversally in the
interconnectivity between the ecosystems present in the area of influence.
Various forcing factors have been identified for both ecosystems based on information
compiled from the different components analyzed in this Chapter and in the ecosystem relationships
presented in the concept maps. These forcing factors can be of physical, chemical,
biological, natural and/or anthropogenic, depending on the characteristics of each ecosystem.
